Combat as cosmogony (Ch34) — the once-and-done order-establishing structure of Marduk's combat with Tiamat in *Enūma Eliš*, taken in Ch34 as the chaoskampf typology's strong-cosmogonic anchor, is the structural-comparative foil against which the Bida narrative's covenant-broken-and-prosperity-ended structure is read in §I above as inverted *at the level of outcome* (slaying ends rather than establishes prosperity) without being inverted at the level of the underlying categorical apparatus (the Soninke framing runs on covenant-and-consequence rather than on cosmos-versus-chaos).
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C2. Combat as sovereignty (Ch35) — the sovereignty-establishing structure of Baal's combat with Yamm and Zeus's combat with Typhon, taken in Ch35 as the typology's strong-sovereignty anchor, is contrasted in §I above with the sovereignty-ending structure of the Bida slaying: where Baal's and Zeus's slayings establish or secure divine sovereignty, Bida's slaying ends the sovereignty-of-Wagadu that the covenant supported.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C8. Refusal and appropriation (Ch41) — the area-specialist-first discipline applied at §I above, demonstrating how the covenant-broken framing precedes any inverse-chaoskampf overlay, prepares the C8 chapter's meta-question of what comparativism owes the area-specialists when the typology's categories are contested; Bida is the inversion problem's anchor case and the appropriation problem's milder-end exemplar.

=== II. The full refusal — Mwindo and the Nyanga maturation-through-hubris framing

The Nyanga oral epic of Mwindo, recorded in 1956 from the named narrator Shé-Kárisi Candi Rureke at Bese on the upper Oso river in the Kivu region of what was then the eastern Belgian Congo and published in Daniel Biebuyck and Kahombo C. Mateene's *The Mwindo Epic from the Banyanga (Zaire)* (University of California Press, 1969) as the standard English-language critical edition and translation, presents the dragon-slaying not as the establishing act of cosmic order or of the hero's sovereignty but as an act of hubristic over-reach for which the sky-god Nkuba punishes the hero — a structural and moral feature of the recorded performance that Biebuyck and Mateene 1969 and Belcher 1999 (*Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Mwindo) both develop as the load-bearing didactic register of the Kirimu episode within the wider epic.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the 1956 Bese recording from Shé-Kárisi Candi Rureke as the chapter's anchor text and on Biebuyck and Mateene 1969 as the standard English-language critical edition and translation; on the dragon-slaying's narrative-structural function as hubristic over-reach that the sky-god Nkuba punishes; Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Mwindo, on the maturation-through-hubris reading as the area-specialist consensus reading of the episode's didactic register; per glossary 'Mwindo', 'Kirimu', 'Nkuba' first_mention_glosses on the principal protagonists.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The Nyanga tradition's own framing of the Mwindo epic — as Biebuyck and Mateene 1969 develop it from the recorded performance and as Belcher 1999 reads it within the comparative African-oral-epic apparatus — is a connected biographical-and-moral narrative of the hero's life, not a cosmogonic narrative: the cosmos is already in place when the epic begins, layered into Nkuba's sky-realm above, Muisa's underworld below, and Iyangura's river-and-water realm laterally; Mwindo's miraculous birth, his disputes with his father Shemwindo (chief of Tubondo, hostile to the son's birth), his journeys across the human and spirit worlds, his testing by the powers above and below, and his eventual chastened return as a properly-formed chief organise the epic from beginning to end, and the hero's biographical-and-moral arc is the compositional centre rather than the establishment of any cosmographic frame.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the Nyanga tradition's framing of the *Mwindo Epic* as a connected biographical-and-moral narrative rather than a cosmogonic narrative, on the layered cosmography (Nkuba's sky-realm above, Muisa's underworld below, Iyangura's river-realm laterally) within which the hero's journeys unfold, and on the hero's biographical-and-moral arc as the compositional centre; Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, translators' introduction on the same biographical-arc framing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The middle-epic passages of the recorded performance present Mwindo's accumulating power as *increasingly dangerous*: the hero's aunts and various other figures along the way warn him repeatedly that his power is exceeding what is permitted, the formulaic refrains and direct narrative comments register the powers above and below as watching the hero's career with a view to the eventual moment at which the correction will fall, and the audience is prepared by these passages to read the Kirimu episode when it arrives as the moment for which the foreshadowing pattern has been preparing — not as a triumph but as an act for which the hero will be answerable.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the middle-epic foreshadowing pattern in the recorded performance — formulaic refrains and direct narrative comments registering Mwindo's accumulating power as dangerous, his aunts' warnings, and the announced expectation that the hubris will require correction; on the audience-preparation function of the foreshadowing for the Kirimu episode; Belcher 1999, ch. on Mwindo, on the foreshadowing-of-correction pattern as load-bearing for the epic's middle sections.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

Kirimu — the dragon at the chapter's narrative centre — is presented in the recorded performance as a being of the spirit world whose existence is part of the cosmographic equilibrium between the human and spirit realms, not as a cosmic adversary whose defeat is the cosmos's establishment; the dragon's narrative-structural role is to be the cosmographic counterpart whose presence the human world must respect, and the hero's killing of him is registered in the recorded performance — by formulaic refrains and direct narrative comments — as an *unsanctioned act of human power against the spirit world* rather than as an act of cosmos-ordering.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on Kirimu's narrative-structural role as a being of the spirit world whose existence is part of the cosmographic equilibrium, on the killing's registration in the recorded performance as an unsanctioned act of human power against the spirit world, and on the moral-cosmographic principle (developed in the recorded text's formulaic refrains and direct narrative comments) that such an act demands a counter-act from the powers above to restore the equilibrium; Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, Kirimu episode passages on the same transgression-not-triumph framing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The killing itself, in the recorded performance, is completed without any of the order-establishing apparatus that the chaoskampf typology expects at this point: there is no narrative passage in which the killing of the dragon establishes the hero's sovereignty, no passage in which the cosmos is reorganised around the dragon's defeat, no festival celebrating the killing as cosmic-order-establishing — the hero kills the dragon, takes the carcass or pieces of it back toward the human settlements, and the narrative immediately turns to Nkuba's response-passage; the *structural absence* of order-establishing apparatus is itself one of the recorded performance's load-bearing features for the Nyanga tradition's reading of the episode.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the killing's completion with no immediate triumphal conclusion — no sovereignty-establishment passage, no cosmos-reorganisation passage, no order-establishing festival — and on the structural absence of order-establishing apparatus as itself a load-bearing feature of the recorded performance; on the immediate narrative turn from the killing to Nkuba's response.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

Nkuba's response is immediate, deliberate, and morally-motivated: the sky-god descends in lightning, seizes the hero out of the human world, and conveys him by lightning from the forest-and-village landscape upward into the celestial realm, where Mwindo is subjected to a sequence of trials and humiliations — exposure to extreme heat and cold, compulsion to endure the celestial powers' direct presence in a way that strips him of his heroic confidence, and explicit instruction about the limits of his power and the necessity of recognising the cosmographic authority of the powers above — that the recorded performance presents not as arbitrary cruelty but as a structurally-necessary correction of the hero's hubristic over-reach.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on Nkuba's lightning-descent and seizing of the hero, on the celestial-realm trials and humiliations (exposure to extreme heat and cold, compulsion to endure the celestial powers' direct presence, explicit instruction about the limits of the hero's power), and on the moral-didactic register of the punishment as a structurally-necessary correction rather than as arbitrary cruelty; per glossary 'Nkuba' first_mention_gloss on Nkuba as Nyanga sky-god who punishes Mwindo for the killing of Kirimu.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The conditions Nkuba imposes on Mwindo's return to the human world are explicit, named, and binding within the recorded performance's framing: the hero is required to renounce certain practices, to accept the binding force of the cosmographic limits the celestial powers have set, and to commit to a chastened mode of conduct as a properly-formed chief on his return — and the hero's eventual restoration to the forest-and-village landscape is conditional and supervised, registered in the recorded performance as a return on the celestial powers' terms rather than as a triumphant homecoming on the hero's own terms.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the conditions Nkuba imposes on Mwindo's return — explicit, named, and binding renunciations and commitments — and on the hero's restoration to the human world as conditional and supervised rather than as a triumphant homecoming; on the chastened character of the return as structurally load-bearing for the episode's resolution alongside the celestial humiliation itself.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The post-Kirimu narrative continuation in the wider epic presents the chastened hero as at last in a position to assume the chiefly office for which the early-and-middle epic's father-son conflict has been preparing him — the chiefly succession being narratively conditional on the hero's having undergone the celestial humiliation and on his having accepted the cosmographic limits the celestial powers have imposed — and the moral-developmental shape of the epic's overall arc is therefore the *maturation-through-humiliation* pattern that Biebuyck and Mateene 1969 identify as the narrative's didactic kernel and that Belcher 1999 reads as the load-bearing feature distinguishing the Mwindo material from the Eurasian combat-myth corpus.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the post-Kirimu narrative continuation, on the chiefly succession's narrative framing as conditional on the celestial humiliation, and on the maturation-through-humiliation pattern as the narrative's didactic kernel; Biebuyck & Mateene 1969 and Belcher 1999, ch. on Mwindo, on the maturation-through-humiliation reading as the load-bearing feature distinguishing the Mwindo material from the Eurasian combat-myth corpus.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Mwindo

The maturation-through-hubris framing has, like the covenant-broken framing of §I, no need of the chaoskampf category to make sense of itself: the hero's biographical arc, his accumulation of power, the foreshadowing of correction, the killing as transgressive over-reach, the celestial humiliation, the chastened return, and the chiefly succession are all internal to the Nyanga tradition's own moral-didactic and reciprocal-balance registers, and a hearer who reads the recorded performance within those registers does not require any concept of "primordial chaos" or "cosmos-establishing combat" to follow what happens or to take the moral point — the narrative is *complete* within its own categories, and the chaoskampf reading of the Kirimu episode is an *additional* analytic move performed from outside the Nyanga tradition by a reader whose reading apparatus has been formed elsewhere.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the completeness of the maturation-through-hubris framing within the Nyanga tradition's own categories and on the chaoskampf reading as an additional analytic move performed from outside the tradition; Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, translators' introduction and Kirimu episode passages on the self-sufficiency of the Nyanga tradition's framing for the recorded performance; Ballentine 2015, on the methodological discipline of separating area-specialist framings from comparative-typology overlays.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ballentine-2015 ; loc=on the methodological discipline of separating area-specialist framings from comparative typology

The Mwindo / Kirimu episode is therefore not an *inversion* of the chaoskampf typology in the sense of Bida (where the *outcome* is inverted: slaying ends rather than establishes prosperity), but an *inversion of moral valence*: the killing is presented as hubris rather than as order-making, the dragon's death is registered as a transgression that demands correction rather than as the cosmogonic or sovereignty-establishing act, and the moral evaluation is the precise contrary of the chaoskampf typology's standard valuation of the hero's slaying — and this moral inversion is more pointed than Bida's outcome inversion because it leaves no narrative-structural space for reading the killing as a chaoskampf event whose moral framing has been negotiated downward; the killing is the episode at which a *categorically different* reading of the dragon-killing motif is in play.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the moral-valence inversion of the Mwindo / Kirimu episode relative to the chaoskampf typology — the killing as hubris rather than as order-making, the death as transgression rather than as cosmos-establishment, and the moral evaluation as the precise contrary of the chaoskampf valuation; on the moral inversion as more pointed than Bida's outcome inversion in leaving no narrative-structural space for negotiated chaoskampf reading; per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml comparative_argument on the Mwindo material as the chapter's case of full refusal of the chaos-order narrative.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The chapter's reading of the Mwindo material as a *refusal* of the chaoskampf typology's moral-cosmographic apparatus, rather than as a member of an "inverse chaoskampf" subtype within that apparatus, follows the area-specialist tradition that Biebuyck and Mateene 1969 anchor and that Belcher 1999 develops within the comparative African-oral-epic apparatus: the Nyanga tradition's framing — maturation-through-hubris, reciprocal balance between human and spirit worlds, the sky-god's authority over the hero — is the tradition's own categorical apparatus, and reading the Kirimu episode as the inverse of a Eurasian combat-myth pattern would be to subordinate the Nyanga categorical apparatus to a comparative typology developed for materials whose categorical apparatus is structurally different.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the area-specialist reading of the Mwindo material as refusing the chaoskampf typology's moral-cosmographic apparatus rather than fitting within it as an "inverse chaoskampf" instance; Biebuyck & Mateene 1969 and Belcher 1999 on the Nyanga tradition's categorical apparatus (maturation-through-hubris, reciprocal balance, sky-god authority); per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" on the methodological status of comparativist-typological readings as additional moves rather than as primary readings of non-Eurasian materials.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Mwindo

[APPROPRIATION-FLAG: the area-specialist objection registered here is that the Mwindo / Kirimu episode's *own* framing within the Nyanga oral tradition is maturation-through-hubris — a moral-didactic and reciprocal-balance framing in which the hero's killing of the dragon is registered as an unsanctioned act of human power against the spirit world that the sky-god Nkuba punishes by lightning-seizing and celestial humiliation, and in which the killing's narrative function is to set up the hero's chastening and his eventual chastened assumption of the chiefly office — and not "inversion of cosmogony", a frame imported from the comparative typology of Eurasian combat-myth scholarship and applied to the Nyanga material from outside; the objection is more pointed than the Bida case (which is an inversion of *outcome* within a frame the Soninke tradition does not employ) because the Nyanga case is an inversion of *moral valence* on top of a categorical apparatus that the Nyanga tradition does not employ either, leaving the chaoskampf reading no negotiable space within the Nyanga tradition's own framing. | named_specialists: Daniel Biebuyck and Kahombo C. Mateene (*The Mwindo Epic from the Banyanga (Zaire)*, 1969, Kirimu episode and following celestial-journey passages; in sources.yaml as biebuyck-mateene-mwindo) are the principal area-specialist anchors for the maturation-through-hubris reading; Stephen Belcher (*Epic Traditions of Africa*, 1999, ch. on Mwindo; in sources.yaml as belcher-epic) is the principal comparative African-oral-epic synthesis anchor; the wider Nyanga-area area-specialist tradition associated with Biebuyck's later *Hero and Chief: Epic Literature from the Banyanga* (1978; named in body prose at Ch29 per the named-but-flagged secondary-anchor pattern) supplies the wider area-specialist scholarly tradition within which the Mwindo material is read. | tier_implication: scope.md classifies the Mwindo material as CONTRASTIVE-tier (full) precisely because the area-specialist framing refuses the chaoskampf typology's moral-cosmographic apparatus rather than accommodating it as an inverse-chaoskampf instance, and the contrastive-tier classification is itself a recognition of the area-specialists' priority and of the "resistance is the data" discipline that the Mwindo material exemplifies. | forwards_to: this APPROPRIATION-FLAG is engaged at length in the present comparative chapter (Ch40, the inversion problem) as the most pointed of the chapter's cases, and again in Ch41 (the refusal-and-appropriation problem), where the meta-question of what comparativism owes the area-specialists when the typology's own categories are refused is the chapter's organising question; the Mwindo case is the strongest case in the present book for the appropriation-protocol's substantive bite.]

The Mwindo episode's recording-history situation is itself worth registering at this point because it bears on the methodological discipline §II applies: the recorded performance is a single 1956 field encounter at Bese with the named narrator Shé-Kárisi Candi Rureke, made within the late-Belgian-colonial-period ethnographic context of the eastern Belgian Congo, with no parallel deep ethnographic-recording corpus across decades to triangulate against and with the colonial-ethnographic compositional-context filtering that the situation introduces — and current Mwindo-studies scholarship including Biebuyck's own subsequent reflective treatments and Belcher 1999 reads the 1956 Rureke text critically as a starting point rather than as a transparently-faithful record of pre-colonial Nyanga performance, a methodological caution that is itself part of the area-specialist citation policy this chapter applies.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the 1956 Bese recording's textual situation — single field encounter, late-Belgian-colonial-period context, no parallel deep ethnographic-recording corpus, colonial-ethnographic compositional-context filtering — and on the critical-reading discipline applied by current Mwindo-studies scholarship; Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, translators' introduction on the recording-history situation; Belcher 1999, ch. on Mwindo, on the critical-reading discipline.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The methodological pay-off of presenting the maturation-through-hubris framing first, before invoking the chaoskampf-and-its-inversion overlay, is — as in §I — that the comparison the chapter performs is *more* precise rather than less: the chaoskampf typology is not asked to do work the Nyanga tradition refuses, the area-specialists' framing is not subordinated to a comparative apparatus the recorded performance does not employ, and the comparison between the Mwindo material and the Eurasian combat-myth corpus can proceed at the level of *categorical difference* (the Nyanga moral-cosmographic apparatus is structurally different from the chaoskampf moral-cosmographic apparatus) without forcing the comparison through a common categorical apparatus that would flatten the Nyanga material's distinctive contribution.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the methodological pay-off of presenting the maturation-through-hubris framing first; Ballentine 2015, on the methodological discipline of separating area-specialist framings from comparative-typology overlays as the route to more precise comparison; per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml special_instructions on the chapter's theoretical contribution being that allowing the area-specialists to speak first does not destroy the comparison but makes it more precise.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ballentine-2015 ; loc=on the methodological discipline of separating area-specialist framings from comparative typology

The contrast between Bida (§I) and Mwindo (§II) is itself instructive for the chapter's typological work: where Bida's case is the inversion of the *outcome* of the chaoskampf typology (slaying ends rather than establishes prosperity) within a covenant-broken framing that does not employ chaoskampf categories, Mwindo's case is the inversion of the *moral valence* within a maturation-through-hubris framing that not only does not employ chaoskampf categories but explicitly refuses the moral evaluation that the typology assigns to the hero's slaying — and the two cases together demonstrate that the typology's "inversion" subtype is not a single phenomenon but a cluster of structurally-different area-specialist framings that the comparativist's "inverse chaoskampf" label flattens together at the cost of the area-specialists' precision.footnote:[Per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml comparative_argument on the contrast between Bida's outcome-inversion and Mwindo's moral-valence-inversion as itself instructive for the chapter's typological work, and on the demonstration that the typology's "inversion" subtype is a cluster of structurally-different area-specialist framings rather than a single phenomenon; per chapters/28-bida-wagadu.claims.approved.adoc and chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the structurally-different area-specialist framings of the two cases.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ballentine-2015 ; loc=on the typological discipline of separating area-specialist framings from comparative typology

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C1. Combat as cosmogony (Ch34) — the order-establishing-by-combat structure of Marduk's combat with Tiamat, taken in Ch34 as the chaoskampf typology's strong-cosmogonic anchor, is the structural-comparative foil against which the Mwindo / Kirimu episode is read in §II above as a moral-valence-inverted case in which the killing is hubris rather than order-establishment; the Mwindo case is the chapter's most pointed refusal of the C1 subtype's moral-cosmographic apparatus.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C5. The IE HERO SLAY SERPENT formula (Ch38) — the IE-formula reading of dragon-slaying as a heroic deed-template that establishes the hero's standing, taken in Ch38 as the IE-comparative reading of Sigurd-Fáfnir, is contrasted in §II above with the Mwindo case in which the dragon-slaying establishes nothing (the hero is punished, not vindicated, by the slaying); the contrast prepares the §III treatment of Sigurd as the partial case at which the IE-formula reading and the long-arc tragedy of the cycle pull in different directions.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C8. Refusal and appropriation (Ch41) — the Mwindo case is the present chapter's strongest case for the appropriation-protocol's substantive bite, and the C8 chapter develops the meta-question of what comparativism owes the area-specialists when the typology's own moral-cosmographic apparatus is refused; the Mwindo case is the inversion problem's pointed-end exemplar and the appropriation problem's anchor case.

=== III. The partial case — Sigurd, the IE formula, and the curse on the gold

The Old Norse Eddic Sigurd-cycle, preserved principally in the *Fáfnismál* of the Codex Regius (GKS 2365 4to, c. 1270 CE) — the second of the three closely-grouped Sigurd-cycle poems in the Codex (with *Reginsmál* preceding and *Sigrdrífumál* following) — and cited in the present chapter through Carolyne Larrington's revised Oxford World's Classics translation of *The Poetic Edda* (Oxford University Press, 2014), presents a partial case for the inversion problem: Watkins 1995 reads the *Fáfnismál*'s diction as the strongest philological reflex of the reconstructed Indo-European poetic formula HERO SLAY SERPENT in the entire Indo-European corpus, but the long-arc tragedy of the Sigurd-cycle complicates the order-establishing-by-combat reading that the formula's surface structure would suggest.footnote:[Per chapters/19-ie-reflexes.claims.approved.adoc on the *Fáfnismál* (Codex Regius GKS 2365 4to, c. 1270 CE) as the principal primary text for the Sigurd-Fáfnir narrative and on Larrington 2014 (rev. Oxford World's Classics) as the chapter's standard English-language translation; Watkins 1995, *How to Kill a Dragon: Aspects of Indo-European Poetics*, ch. 42 on the *Fáfnismál* diction as the strongest philological reflex of the reconstructed IE poetic formula HERO SLAY SERPENT in the entire IE corpus; per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml structure on Sigurd as the chapter's partial case at which the IE-formula reading and the long-arc tragedy of the cycle pull in different directions.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 42
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál

The narrative core of the *Fáfnismál* is the killing of the dragon Fáfnir by the young hero Sigurd, son of Sigmund of the Völsung lineage and foster-son of the smith Regin (Fáfnir's brother): Sigurd ambushes the dragon by digging a pit on the dragon's path to the water, lying in wait below, and thrusting upward into Fáfnir's underbelly with the sword Gram (forged by Regin from the fragments of Sigmund's broken sword) as the dragon passes over the pit; the killing-stroke is delivered as the central act around which the poem's dialogue is organised, with the bulk of the *Fáfnismál* given over to the post-killing wisdom-dialogue between the dying dragon and the hero rather than to extended combat-narrative.footnote:[Per chapters/19-ie-reflexes.claims.approved.adoc on the *Fáfnismál*'s narrative core — Sigurd's lineage and Regin's foster-role, the pit-and-ambush mechanism, the upward thrust with Gram into Fáfnir's underbelly, and the central placement of the killing-stroke around which the poem's dialogue is organised; Larrington 2014, *Fáfnismál* opening and middle stanzas, on the killing-stroke and on the post-killing wisdom-dialogue's bulk.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál opening

Watkins's IE-formula reading reads the *Fáfnismál*'s diction as a direct philological reflex of the reconstructed Indo-European poetic formula HERO SLAY SERPENT — the formula reconstructed across Vedic Indra-versus-Vṛtra material (where Watkins 1995 establishes the formula's reconstructed shape from the ​​Ṛgvedic diction), Greek and Iranian comparanda, and the Old Norse Eddic *Fáfnismál* itself — and the Eddic poet's choice of verb, object, and syntactic frame at the killing-stroke is the strongest philological evidence Watkins 1995 marshals for the formula's continued productivity at the diction-level in early-medieval Old Norse poetry; the Sigurd-Fáfnir killing is therefore an IE-poetic-formula reflex at the level of *language*, not just at the level of narrative motif.footnote:[Per chapters/19-ie-reflexes.claims.approved.adoc on Watkins's IE-formula reading of the *Fáfnismál* diction as a direct philological reflex of the reconstructed IE formula HERO SLAY SERPENT; Watkins 1995, ch. 42, on the Eddic poet's verb, object, and syntactic frame at the killing-stroke as the strongest philological evidence for the formula's productivity at the diction-level in early-medieval Old Norse poetry; on the Sigurd-Fáfnir killing as an IE-poetic-formula reflex at the level of language rather than just at the level of narrative motif.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 42

The IE-formula reading, taken on its own, reads the killing as a heroic deed-template event in which the hero's IE-typological standing is established by the slaying — and the surface narrative supports the reading: the *Fáfnismál* indicates that Sigurd, immediately after the killing, gains the speech of birds by tasting the dragon's heart's blood, kills the treacherous Regin in turn (warned by the birds of Regin's intent), takes Fáfnir's hoard onto his horse Grani, and proceeds to the next stage of his career (the encounter with the valkyrie Sigrdrífa / Brynhild in the *Sigrdrífumál*); the surface trajectory looks like vindication-of-the-hero by the slaying.footnote:[Per chapters/19-ie-reflexes.claims.approved.adoc on the post-killing surface trajectory — heart-roasting, blood-tasting, finger-burn, bird-speech, the nuthatches' warning, Sigurd's killing of Regin, the loading of the hoard onto Grani, and the bridge to the Sigrdrífa / Brynhild encounter; Larrington 2014, *Fáfnismál* (heart-roasting and bird-speech) and concluding stanzas, on the surface vindication-of-the-hero trajectory.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál (heart-roasting and bird-speech)

But the long-arc tragedy of the Sigurd-cycle complicates the surface reading: the gold of Andvari that Loki extorts to pay Hreidmar's wergild for the killing of Otr (Hreidmar's son and Fáfnir's and Regin's brother) is *cursed* by Andvari at the moment of the extortion, and the curse drives the entire downstream cycle — the death of Hreidmar at Fáfnir's hands, Fáfnir's transformation into a dragon by his obsessive guardianship of the cursed gold, Regin's treachery toward Sigurd, Sigurd's eventual death at the hands of Gunnar's brother Gutthorm in the Burgundian episode, Brynhild's death, and the wider Burgundian disasters — and the killing of the dragon, far from establishing Sigurd's prosperity, is the *acquisition of the curse* that destroys him and the people around him.footnote:[Per chapters/19-ie-reflexes.claims.approved.adoc on the long-arc tragedy of the Sigurd-cycle from *Reginsmál* through *Fáfnismál*, *Sigrdrífumál*, and the Burgundian poems to Sigurd's death — the curse on Andvari's gold as the load-bearing motif driving the cycle from the killing of the dragon to Sigurd's own death and the Burgundian disasters; Larrington 2014, *Reginsmál* and *Fáfnismál* on the curse's establishment; on the killing as the acquisition of the curse rather than the establishment of prosperity.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Reginsmál; Fáfnismál

The dying Fáfnir's own warning to Sigurd within the *Fáfnismál*'s post-killing dialogue is itself part of the *Fáfnismál*'s framing: Fáfnir, mortally wounded, asks Sigurd's name, warns him of the curse on the gold, and supplies cosmological-and-genealogical lore in the wisdom-dialogue register that the *Vafþrúðnismál* and *Grímnismál* establish elsewhere in the Eddic corpus, and the warning-of-the-curse is built into the killing-poem itself — the narrative does not require external commentary to register the killing as ambiguous; the dragon's own speech registers the act's tragic shape in the moment of its completion.footnote:[Per chapters/19-ie-reflexes.claims.approved.adoc on the post-killing wisdom-dialogue between Sigurd and the dying Fáfnir — Fáfnir's asking of Sigurd's name, the warning of the curse, the cosmological-and-genealogical lore in the wisdom-dialogue register — and on the warning-of-the-curse as built into the killing-poem itself; Larrington 2014, *Fáfnismál* stanzas (post-killing dialogue), on the wisdom-dialogue register paralleled in *Vafþrúðnismál* and *Grímnismál*.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál stanzas (post-killing dialogue)

The IE-formula reading and the curse-on-the-gold reading are not in straightforward conflict: the IE-formula reading is a *philological* claim about the diction of the killing-stroke, and the curse-on-the-gold reading is a *narrative* claim about the long-arc trajectory of the cycle, and the two readings are at different analytic levels; but the readings *are* in tension at the typological level, where the IE-formula's standard interpretation as a heroic-deed-template-establishing-the-hero's-standing reading runs against the cycle's narrative trajectory in which the killing acquires the curse that destroys the hero.footnote:[Per chapters/19-ie-reflexes.claims.approved.adoc on the analytic-level distinction between Watkins's philological IE-formula reading and the narrative-level long-arc tragedy reading, and on the typological-level tension between the heroic-deed-template-establishing-the-hero's-standing reading and the curse-acquiring-and-hero-destroying reading; Watkins 1995, ch. 42, on the IE-formula reading; Larrington 2014, on the long-arc tragedy.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 42
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Sigurd-cycle long arc

The Sigurd case is therefore a *partial* inversion case: the killing is at one analytic level a paradigm IE dragon-slaying (Watkins's strongest philological reflex; the heroic-deed-template at the diction-level), and at another analytic level the acquisition of the curse that destroys the hero (the cycle's long-arc tragedy; the killing as the trigger of the cycle's catastrophic outcome) — and the case sits on the edge between the chaoskampf typology's order-establishing reading and the inversion-problem cases of §§I–II in a way that neither vindicates the typology's standard reading nor fully refuses it.footnote:[Per chapters/19-ie-reflexes.claims.approved.adoc on the Sigurd case as a partial inversion case sitting on the edge between the chaoskampf typology's order-establishing reading and the inversion-problem cases of §§I–II of the present chapter; per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml structure on Sigurd as the partial case at which the IE-formula reading and the long-arc tragedy of the cycle pull in different directions.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 42

The Sigurd case is also methodologically distinctive within the chapter: where Bida (§I), Mwindo (§II), Pariacaca (§IV), and Susanoo (§V) are non-Indo-European cases at which the area-specialist citation policy of scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" is in force, the Sigurd case is internal to the IE-comparative tradition that Watkins 1995 anchors and that Ch19 of the present book develops as the IE-reflexes chapter — and the appropriation-protocol's discipline of presenting the area-specialist framing first does not apply in the same way, because the Eddic philological tradition and the IE-comparative tradition are part of the *same* scholarly apparatus rather than being foreign to it; the Sigurd case is therefore *not* a case where comparativism imposes its categories on an outside tradition, and the inversion-problem reading at §III is correspondingly different in its methodological warrant from the readings at §§I–II and §§IV–V.footnote:[Per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" on the area-specialist citation requirement applying to non-Eurasian materials and to traditions outside the IE-comparative apparatus; per chapters/19-ie-reflexes.claims.approved.adoc on the Sigurd-cycle's place internal to the IE-comparative tradition that Watkins 1995 anchors; per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml structure on Sigurd as a case methodologically distinct from §§I–II and §§IV–V because the Eddic philological tradition is internal to the IE-comparative apparatus rather than foreign to it.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

[INFERENCE: the Sigurd case's appropriate placement in the inversion-problem chapter is as a *partial* case rather than as either a vindication of the chaoskampf-typology reading (in which the killing establishes the hero's standing) or a full inversion case (in which the killing has the opposite valence): the philological IE-formula reading is robust and is the strongest evidence for the formula's productivity in early-medieval Old Norse poetry, but the long-arc tragedy of the cycle complicates the reading at the narrative level, and the appropriate registration of the case is as evidence that the chaoskampf typology and its inversion are themselves graded categories with edges rather than discrete bins. | basis: Watkins 1995 ch. 42 supplies the IE-formula reading at the philological level; Larrington 2014's *Fáfnismál* and the surrounding Codex Regius poems supply the long-arc tragedy at the narrative level; the brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml structure explicitly characterises Sigurd as the chapter's partial case. | risk: the partial-case classification depends on accepting both the philological IE-formula reading and the long-arc tragedy reading at the analytic levels at which Watkins and Larrington respectively present them; alternative readings have been advanced (some Eddic scholars read the long-arc tragedy as compositional layering rather than as a unified cycle, which would weaken the curse-acquisition reading), and the partial-case classification is the chapter's reading rather than the only available scholarly position.]

The Sigurd case's contribution to the chapter's typological work is therefore not the demonstration of a third inversion-pattern (in addition to Bida's outcome-inversion and Mwindo's moral-valence-inversion), but the demonstration that the chaoskampf typology and its inversion-subtype are themselves *graded categories* with edges — that within the IE-comparative tradition where the typology's reading apparatus has its strongest claim to analytic traction, the case at which the philological evidence is strongest is also a case at which the narrative outcome resists the typology's standard reading at the long-arc level — and the Sigurd case is therefore the chapter's evidence that the inversion problem is not confined to the area-specialist-objection cases of §§I–II and §§IV–V but is internal to the IE-comparative tradition itself.footnote:[Per chapters/19-ie-reflexes.claims.approved.adoc on the Sigurd-cycle's positioning within the IE-comparative tradition and on the case's complication of the chaoskampf typology's order-establishing reading at the long-arc narrative level; per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml comparative_argument on the Sigurd case's contribution to the chapter's typological work as evidence that the typology and its inversion-subtype are graded categories with edges rather than discrete bins; Watkins 1995 ch. 42 on the philological-evidence strength; Larrington 2014 on the long-arc tragedy.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 42

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C5. The IE HERO SLAY SERPENT formula (Ch38) — the IE-formula reading anchored by Watkins 1995 ch. 42 and developed in Ch38 as the present book's principal IE-comparative chapter is presented in §III above as the philological-level reading of the Sigurd-Fáfnir case, with the long-arc tragedy reading complicating the IE-formula's standard interpretation as a heroic-deed-template-establishing-the-hero's-standing event; the §III treatment is the inversion-problem chapter's IE-internal complement to the area-specialist-objection cases of §§I–II and §§IV–V.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C2. Combat as sovereignty (Ch35) — the sovereignty-establishing reading of dragon-slaying as a heroic-deed-template event that establishes the hero's standing, taken in Ch35 as the typology's strong-sovereignty anchor, is the structural-comparative foil against which the Sigurd case is read in §III above as a partial inversion in which the slaying acquires the curse rather than establishing standing; the contrast is at the long-arc narrative level rather than at the philological-diction level.

=== IV. The area-specialist refusal — Pariacaca, the amaru, and the wak'a-succession framing

The early-colonial Andean *Huarochirí Manuscript*, a Quechua-language compilation conventionally dated to approximately 1608 CE and held at the Biblioteca Nacional de España in Madrid as MS 3169 within materials connected to the early-seventeenth-century anti-idolatry campaign of the Cuzco-born secular priest Francisco de Ávila in the Lima archdiocese, preserves the principal Andean narrative cycle within the present book's scope: the cycle of the storm-_wak'a_ Pariacaca, born as a clutch of falcon-eggs on Mount Condorcoto, who opposes and defeats the rival _wak'a_ Huallallo Carhuincho and a sequence of further rivals across the Huarochirí region's high-altitude landscape, with the serpentine _amaru_ figuring across the cycle as an ally-monster summoned by defeated rivals rather than as a primordial cosmic adversary.footnote:[Per chapters/26-pariacaca-amaru.claims.approved.adoc on the *Huarochirí Manuscript* (c. 1608 CE; Biblioteca Nacional de España, Madrid, MS 3169) as the chapter's principal primary source, on the Pariacaca cycle's distribution across chs. 6, 8, and 21 of the manuscript, on Pariacaca's birth as a clutch of falcon-eggs on Mount Condorcoto, and on the *amaru* as ally-monster summoned by defeated rivals rather than as a primordial cosmic adversary; Salomon and Urioste 1991, *The Huarochirí Manuscript: A Testament of Ancient and Colonial Andean Religion* (Austin: University of Texas Press, 1991; ISBN 9780292730533), translators' introduction and chs. 6, 8, 21, on the same; per glossary 'Pariacaca', 'amaru', 'wak'a' first_mention_glosses on the principal terms.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript chs. 6, 8, 21 (Pariacaca and amaru passages)

The area-specialist reading of the cycle, anchored in the present book by Salomon and Urioste 1991 and developed within the wider current Andean-studies scholarly tradition by Frank Salomon and George Urioste themselves (in their 1991 critical edition's translators' introduction), Gérald Taylor in his Lima IFEA critical edition of the *Huarochirí Manuscript* (named in body prose at Ch26 of the present book per the named-but-flagged secondary-anchor pattern), and Carolyn Dean on Andean ritual-political imagination (named in body prose at Ch26 per the same pattern), is _wak'a_-succession: the cycle's narrative work is the displacement of one hegemonic regional _wak'a_ (Huallallo Carhuincho) by another (Pariacaca) within an already-existing _wak'a_-cosmography rather than the establishment of cosmic order ex novo from the primordial cosmos-versus-disorder opposition that the chaoskampf typology presupposes.footnote:[Per chapters/26-pariacaca-amaru.claims.approved.adoc on the area-specialist reading of the Pariacaca cycle as wak'a-succession rather than as cosmogonic combat, on the named area-specialists Salomon, Urioste, Taylor, and Dean as supplying the reading, and on the wak'a-succession framing as the area-specialist consensus reading; Salomon & Urioste 1991, translators' introduction on the wak'a-succession reading; per glossary 'wak'a' first_mention_gloss on the wak'a-succession-not-chaos-versus-order pattern.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript translators' introduction

The wak'a-succession framing differs from both the Bida outcome-inversion (§I) and the Mwindo moral-valence-inversion (§II) in that it is *not an inversion at all* but a different combat-myth pattern: the Pariacaca cycle does narrate combat between named divine figures (Pariacaca's five-day flood-and-storm engagement with Huallallo Carhuincho in chapter 8 of the manuscript; the engagements with the _amaru_-summoning rivals in subsequent chapters) and a wak'a-political reorganisation does follow from the combat-outcome (Pariacaca takes possession of the eponymous mountain peak; his five sons or brothers take possession of subsidiary peaks and ritual centres; Huallallo Carhuincho's former sphere is reorganised under Pariacaca's authority), but the combat's narrative work is the reorganisation of an already-existing _wak'a_-cosmos rather than the establishment of a cosmos from a primordial disorder.footnote:[Per chapters/26-pariacaca-amaru.claims.approved.adoc on the wak'a-succession framing's structural difference from both outcome-inversion and moral-valence-inversion; on the Pariacaca-Huallallo five-day combat in ch. 8 of the manuscript, on the post-combat geographical settlement (Pariacaca's possession of the eponymous mountain peak, his five sons or brothers taking subsidiary peaks and ritual centres, the reorganisation of Huallallo's former sphere under Pariacaca's authority), and on the combat's narrative work as wak'a-political reorganisation rather than as cosmos-establishment.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript ch. 8

The _amaru_'s narrative role in the cycle is itself the area-specialists' principal evidence for the wak'a-succession reading rather than for any chaoskampf reading: the _amaru_ figures across the cycle as an ally-monster summoned by a defeated rival _wak'a_ in the course of further engagements, with the _amaru_'s narrative function being the discharge of the defeated _wak'a_'s residual combat-capacity rather than the embodiment of a primordial cosmic adversary, and with the _amaru_'s defeat by Pariacaca (or by his sons) typically being a follow-on to the principal _wak'a_-combat rather than the cycle's central engagement.footnote:[Per chapters/26-pariacaca-amaru.claims.approved.adoc on the *amaru*'s narrative role as ally-monster summoned by a defeated rival, on the *amaru*'s function as discharge of the defeated *wak'a*'s residual combat-capacity, and on the *amaru*'s defeat as a follow-on to the principal *wak'a*-combat; per glossary 'amaru' first_mention_gloss on the *amaru* as ally-monster of a defeated rival *wak'a* rather than as primordial chaos.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript chs. 8 and following

The _amaru_'s defeat is itself patterned differently from the kill-and-eradicate pattern of much Eurasian chaoskampf material: the manuscript narrates the _amaru_'s defeat as a cutting, splitting, or driving-into-a-confined-space rather than as comprehensive destruction, with the defeated _amaru_'s body becoming a feature of the landscape (a lake's outline, a watercourse's bend, a stone formation) rather than being reduced to nothing — a pattern that, like the displacement-rather-than-killing of Huallallo Carhuincho, distinguishes the Andean material from the kill-and-eradicate chaoskampf-typology pattern at a load-bearing structural level.footnote:[Per chapters/26-pariacaca-amaru.claims.approved.adoc on the *amaru*'s defeat as cutting, splitting, or driving-into-a-confined-space rather than comprehensive destruction; on the defeated *amaru*'s body becoming a feature of the landscape (a lake's outline, a watercourse's bend, a stone formation); on the parallel between this displacement-rather-than-killing pattern and the wider cycle's wak'a-succession-rather-than-eradication framing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript chs. 8 and following

The Pariacaca case's significance for the inversion-problem chapter is therefore methodologically distinctive: it is *not* an inversion case (the killing does not establish prosperity that is then inverted; the moral valence is not the precise contrary of the chaoskampf reading; the IE-formula reading does not apply) but a case in which the area-specialists' framing does not employ the disorder-versus-order opposition that the chaoskampf typology presupposes, and the comparative observation the chapter takes from §IV is that *not all area-specialist refusals are inversions* — some refusals are categorical, in the sense that the area-specialists' framing employs a different conceptual apparatus (wak'a-succession; landscape-reorganisation rather than cosmos-establishment) that the chaoskampf typology can only register as an absence rather than as an inversion.footnote:[Per chapters/26-pariacaca-amaru.claims.approved.adoc on the Pariacaca case's methodological distinctiveness as a categorical area-specialist refusal rather than as an inversion case; per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml structure on Pariacaca as the chapter's case at which the area-specialist framing does not employ the disorder-versus-order opposition that the chaoskampf typology presupposes; on the comparative observation that not all area-specialist refusals are inversions.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript translators' introduction

[APPROPRIATION-FLAG: the area-specialist objection registered here is that the Pariacaca cycle's *own* framing within the Andean-studies scholarly tradition is wak'a-succession — a wak'a-political-reorganisation framing in which the storm-_wak'a_ Pariacaca's combat with Huallallo Carhuincho and the engagements with the _amaru_-summoning rivals reorganise an already-existing _wak'a_-cosmos rather than establish cosmic order from a primordial cosmos-versus-chaos opposition — and not "inversion of cosmogony" or "inverse chaoskampf"; the objection is, per the brief 26-pariacaca-amaru.yaml special_instructions and per the Ch26 APPROPRIATION-FLAG, milder than the Mwindo objection (the Pariacaca cycle accommodates some combat-myth framing — the combat with Huallallo Carhuincho and the engagements with the _amaru_-summoning rivals are structurally combats — but does not accommodate the chaos-versus-order framing) and milder than the Susanoo objection (which is an objection to the combat-myth framing itself in favour of metallurgical allegory), but the chaos-versus-order framing is precisely what Gunkel-tradition chaoskampf-typology presupposes, and the area-specialists' refusal of that framing is the chapter's load-bearing observation for §IV. | named_specialists: Frank Salomon and George L. Urioste (in their 1991 critical edition of *The Huarochirí Manuscript* [Austin: University of Texas Press, 1991; ISBN 9780292730533]; in sources.yaml as salomon-urioste-huarochiri) are the principal English-language area-specialist anchors for the wak'a-succession reading; Gérald Taylor in his Lima IFEA critical edition of the *Huarochirí Manuscript* (named in body prose per the named-but-flagged secondary-anchor pattern; not currently in sources.yaml) and Carolyn Dean on Andean ritual-political imagination (named in body prose per the same pattern; not currently in sources.yaml) supply the wider Andean-studies area-specialist tradition within which the wak'a-succession reading is set. | tier_implication: scope.md classifies the Pariacaca material as RELATED-tier (full) rather than CORE-tier precisely because the area-specialist framing is wak'a-succession rather than chaoskampf, and the chaoskampf reading would have to be applied from outside the Andean-studies tradition; the tier-classification is itself a recognition of the area-specialists' priority and of the categorical difference between the Andean material's framing and the chaoskampf typology's apparatus. | forwards_to: this APPROPRIATION-FLAG is engaged in the present comparative chapter (Ch40, the inversion problem) as the chapter's case of categorical area-specialist refusal short of inversion, and again in Ch41 (the refusal-and-appropriation problem), where the meta-question of what comparativism owes the area-specialists when the typology's categorical apparatus is refused is the chapter's organising question.]

The Pariacaca case's distinctive contribution to the chapter's typological work is therefore the demonstration that the chaoskampf typology can fail to fit a case not by being inverted but by being *categorically inapplicable*: the cycle's combat-narrative is real, the dragon-figure (the _amaru_) is real, the storm-deity-defeats-serpent surface description is real, but the categorical apparatus that the chaoskampf typology supplies — primordial disorder versus established order, cosmos-establishment-by-combat, kill-and-eradicate of the cosmic adversary — is not the apparatus the Andean-studies tradition employs to read the cycle, and the comparative observation is not "this case inverts the typology" but "this case does not fit the typology because it operates with a different categorical apparatus".footnote:[Per chapters/26-pariacaca-amaru.claims.approved.adoc on the Pariacaca case's demonstration that the chaoskampf typology can fail to fit a case by being categorically inapplicable rather than by being inverted; on the categorical-difference observation as the chapter's load-bearing finding for §IV; Ballentine 2015, on the methodological discipline of recognising when a comparative typology's categorical apparatus does not fit a case rather than treating every misfit as an inversion or a deviation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ballentine-2015 ; loc=on the typological discipline of separating area-specialist framings from comparative typology

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C2. Combat as sovereignty (Ch35) — the sovereignty-establishing reading of dragon-slaying anchored in Ch35 by the Baal and Zeus cases is contrasted in §IV above with the wak'a-succession reading of the Pariacaca cycle; the Andean material does narrate sovereignty-shifts at the wak'a-political level but does not narrate the cosmic-sovereignty-establishment that the C2 typology presupposes.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C8. Refusal and appropriation (Ch41) — the Pariacaca case is the present chapter's case of categorical area-specialist refusal short of inversion, and the C8 chapter develops the meta-question of what comparativism owes the area-specialists when the typology's categorical apparatus is refused; Pariacaca is the inversion problem's mid-range exemplar between the outcome- and moral-valence-inversion cases (Bida, Mwindo) and the metallurgical-allegory categorical refusal (Susanoo).

=== V. The metallurgical case — Susanoo, Yamata no Orochi, and the sword-etiology framing

The early-Japanese imperial chronicles — the *Kojiki* (712 CE) and the *Nihon shoki* (720 CE), compiled within the Nara-period programme of fixing the imperial line's mythic charter in writing under Yamato-court sponsorship — preserve the narrative of Susanoo's defeat of the eight-headed eight-tailed serpent Yamata no Orochi in the land of Izumo, and the chapter cites the *Kojiki* through Donald L. Philippi's translation (Tokyo: University of Tokyo Press / Princeton: Princeton University Press, 1968) at book 1, chs. 19–20 and the metallurgical-allegory area-specialist reading through Nelly Naumann's *Die Mythen des alten Japan* (München: C.H. Beck, 1996).footnote:[Per chapters/23-susanoo-orochi.claims.approved.adoc on the *Kojiki* (712 CE) and the *Nihon shoki* (720 CE) as the two principal early-Japanese imperial chronicles, on Philippi 1968 as the chapter's standard English-language *Kojiki* translation, and on Naumann 1996 as the principal metallurgical-allegory area-specialist anchor; per glossary 'Yamata no Orochi', 'Kusanagi', 'Susanoo' first_mention_glosses on the principal protagonists.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=philippi-kojiki ; loc=Kojiki 1.19-1.20

The narrative core of the *Kojiki* episode is Susanoo's descent to Izumo from the heavenly plain of Takamagahara (after his expulsion for misdeeds), his upstream ascent along the river Hi after observing chopsticks floating downstream (the sign of human habitation upstream), his encounter with the elderly couple Ashinazuchi and Tenazuchi and their daughter Kushinada-hime (the eighth and last surviving daughter, the previous seven having been devoured one each year by the serpent), his trap (an enclosure with eight gates each carrying a vat of eight-fold refined sake, one vat per head), the serpent's intoxication-to-stupor on the sake, Susanoo's dismemberment of the immobilised serpent with his Totsuka-no-Tsurugi sword, his discovery of the sword later named _Kusanagi_ within the serpent's tail, and the presentation of _Kusanagi_ to his sister Amaterasu — with Susanoo subsequently restoring Kushinada-hime from comb-form, marrying her, and constructing the palace at Suga in Izumo as the founding act of the wider Izumo-cycle.footnote:[Per chapters/23-susanoo-orochi.claims.approved.adoc on the *Kojiki* episode's narrative core — Susanoo's expulsion and descent, the river Hi setting, Ashinazuchi and Tenazuchi and Kushinada-hime, the eight-vat sake-trap mechanism, the dismemberment of the immobilised serpent, the discovery of *Kusanagi* in the tail, the presentation of the sword to Amaterasu, and the marriage and palace-construction at Suga; Philippi 1968, *Kojiki*, book 1 chs. 19–20, on the same.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=philippi-kojiki ; loc=Kojiki 1.19

The metallurgical-allegory area-specialist reading, developed in detail by Naumann 1996 in the present book's source-base and supported within the wider current Japanese-studies scholarly tradition by Jun'ichi Isomae, Michael Como, and Herman Ooms (named in body prose at Ch23 of the present book per the named-but-flagged secondary-anchor pattern, with neither Isomae, Como, nor Ooms currently in `sources.yaml` and the present chapter routing no EVIDENCE token through them), reads the serpent as figuring the iron-smelting industry of the Izumo-Hi-river region in the early-historical period and reads the sword's discovery in the tail as figuring the metallurgical emergence of the bladed weapon from the smelting-and-forging process — with the episode's narrative function being sword-etiology and metallurgical-and-political charter rather than cosmogonic or sovereignty-establishing combat.footnote:[Per chapters/23-susanoo-orochi.claims.approved.adoc on the metallurgical-allegory reading anchored by Naumann 1996 and supported by the wider current Japanese-studies scholarly tradition associated with Isomae, Como, and Ooms; Naumann 1996, Orochi chapter, on the serpent as iron-smelting industry of the Izumo-Hi-river region and the sword's discovery in the tail as metallurgical emergence of the bladed weapon; on the episode's narrative function as sword-etiology and metallurgical-political charter rather than as cosmogonic combat.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=naumann-mythen ; loc=Orochi chapter

The metallurgical reading rests on a constellation of narrative-structural features: the river Hi setting (the upper river-courses of mountainous regions are the historical-period sites of iron-sand mining and _tatara_ smelting in Japan); the serpent's red-rusted underside (red rust as the visible signature of iron-bearing minerals along river-courses); the serpent's enormous size spanning eight valleys and eight peaks (the geographic scale of the smelting industry's catchment of fuel-wood and ore); and the chipping of Susanoo's existing sword on the serpent's tail (the metallurgical-quality difference between an older and a newer bladed weapon) — all of them load-bearing for the area-specialists' reading and all of them visible at the level of the recorded narrative-structure of the *Kojiki* episode.footnote:[Per chapters/23-susanoo-orochi.claims.approved.adoc on the metallurgical reading's constellation of narrative-structural features — the river Hi setting as iron-smelting site, the red-rusted underside as iron-bearing-mineral signature, the eight-valleys-and-eight-peaks scale as smelting-industry catchment, the chipping of Susanoo's existing sword on the tail as metallurgical-quality marker; Naumann 1996, Orochi chapter, on the same constellation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=naumann-mythen ; loc=Orochi chapter

The metallurgical reading further connects the Yamata no Orochi episode to the early-historical-period political-economic context of the Yamato court's expansion into Izumo: control of the iron-smelting industry of the Izumo-Hi-river region was a load-bearing material-political stake of the early-imperial period, and the chronicle's narrative-structural placement of the sword-etiology within the Izumo-cycle — Susanoo's defeat of the serpent, his discovery of the sword, his presentation of the sword to Amaterasu, and the sword's status as one of the Three Sacred Treasures of the Japanese imperial regalia — reflects this political-economic stake of the late-seventh-and-early-eighth-century moment of the chronicles' compilation rather than a primordial cosmogonic-combat narrative free of period-specific political content.footnote:[Per chapters/23-susanoo-orochi.claims.approved.adoc on the metallurgical reading's connection to the early-historical-period political-economic context of the Yamato court's expansion into Izumo, on the iron-smelting industry as load-bearing material-political stake, and on the chronicle's narrative-structural placement of the sword-etiology as reflecting the political-economic stake of the late-seventh-and-early-eighth-century moment of the chronicles' compilation; Naumann 1996, Orochi chapter, on the same.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=naumann-mythen ; loc=Orochi chapter

The implication of the metallurgical-allegory reading for the chapter's chaoskampf-typology purpose is direct: if the area-specialist reading is correct that the serpent figures iron-smelting and the sword figures the metallurgical product of smelting, then the episode's load-bearing narrative function is sword-etiology and metallurgical-and-political charter rather than cosmogonic combat — and the chaoskampf label, when applied to the Susanoo / Yamata no Orochi episode, is a 20th-century-Western comparativist move applied to the early-Japanese material from outside rather than an internal feature of the recorded narrative as the area-specialists read it.footnote:[Per chapters/23-susanoo-orochi.claims.approved.adoc on the implication of the metallurgical-allegory reading for the chapter's typological purpose — if the area-specialist reading is correct, the episode's load-bearing function is sword-etiology and metallurgical-political charter rather than cosmogonic combat — and on the chaoskampf label as a 20th-century-Western comparativist move rather than as an internal feature of the recorded narrative; Naumann 1996, Orochi chapter, on the same.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=naumann-mythen ; loc=Orochi chapter

[APPROPRIATION-FLAG: the area-specialist objection registered here is that the Susanoo / Yamata no Orochi episode's *own* framing within the early-Japanese imperial-chronicle tradition and within current Japanese-studies area-specialist scholarship is sword-etiology and metallurgical-and-political charter — the eight-headed serpent figuring the iron-smelting industry of the Izumo-Hi-river region, the discovery of the sword named _Kusanagi_ in the serpent's tail figuring the metallurgical emergence of the bladed weapon from the smelting-and-forging process, the episode's narrative function being the chronicle's account of how the Yamato court came to possess the imperial regalium and to claim authority over the Izumo iron-smelting region — and not "cosmogonic combat" or "chaoskampf"; the objection is the strongest of the four area-specialist objections registered in the present chapter (more pointed than the wak'a-succession objection at §IV because it refuses the *combat-myth framing itself* rather than the chaos-versus-order framing alone), and the area-specialists hold that the chaoskampf label is at best a loose typological-comparative description that fits the surface narrative-structure (storm-deity defeats serpentine adversary, recovers powerful weapon) without engaging the area-specialist account of what the narrative is actually doing in its early-Japanese context. | named_specialists: Nelly Naumann (*Die Mythen des alten Japan*, 1996, Orochi chapter; in sources.yaml as naumann-mythen) is the principal English-and-German-language area-specialist anchor for the metallurgical-allegory reading; Jun'ichi Isomae (named in body prose at Ch23 per the named-but-flagged secondary-anchor pattern; not currently in sources.yaml), Michael Como (named in body prose at Ch23 per the same pattern; not currently in sources.yaml), and Herman Ooms (named in body prose at Ch23 per the same pattern; not currently in sources.yaml) supply the wider current Japanese-studies area-specialist tradition within which the metallurgical-allegory reading is set. | tier_implication: scope.md classifies the Susanoo / Orochi material as RELATED-tier (full) rather than CORE-tier precisely because the area-specialist framing refuses the combat-myth framing itself in favour of metallurgical allegory and sword-etiology, and the chaoskampf reading would have to be applied from outside the Japanese-studies tradition; the tier-classification is itself a recognition of the area-specialists' priority and of the categorical difference between the early-Japanese material's framing and the chaoskampf typology's apparatus. | forwards_to: this APPROPRIATION-FLAG is engaged in the present comparative chapter (Ch40, the inversion problem) as the chapter's strongest area-specialist refusal, and again in Ch41 (the refusal-and-appropriation problem), where the meta-question of what comparativism owes the area-specialists when the *combat-myth framing itself* is refused is the chapter's organising question.]

The Susanoo case's distinctive contribution to the chapter's typological work is therefore the demonstration that the chaoskampf typology can fail to fit a case not at the level of outcome (Bida) or moral valence (Mwindo) or categorical apparatus within combat-myth framing (Pariacaca), but at the *combat-myth framing itself*: if the area-specialists are correct that the load-bearing function of the Susanoo / Orochi episode is sword-etiology and metallurgical-political charter, then the comparative observation is not "this case inverts the typology" or "this case does not fit the typology's categorical apparatus" but "this case is not a combat-myth case at all in the area-specialists' reading, and the comparative typology's application to it is from outside" — and the Susanoo case is the chapter's strongest evidence for the appropriation-protocol's substantive bite at the level of the typology's basic applicability.footnote:[Per chapters/23-susanoo-orochi.claims.approved.adoc on the Susanoo case's demonstration that the chaoskampf typology can fail to fit a case at the level of the combat-myth framing itself, and on the categorical-inapplicability observation as the chapter's load-bearing finding for §V; per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml special_instructions on Naumann, Isomae, Como, and Ooms as the named area-specialists for the metallurgical-allegory reading.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=naumann-mythen ; loc=Orochi chapter

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C1. Combat as cosmogony (Ch34) — the cosmogonic combat-myth framing taken in Ch34 as the typology's strong-cosmogonic anchor is the structural-comparative foil against which the Susanoo / Orochi episode is read in §V above as a case at which the area-specialists refuse the combat-myth framing itself in favour of metallurgical allegory and sword-etiology; the Susanoo case is the chapter's strongest area-specialist refusal of the typology's basic applicability.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C8. Refusal and appropriation (Ch41) — the Susanoo case is the present chapter's strongest area-specialist refusal of the combat-myth framing itself, and the C8 chapter develops the meta-question of what comparativism owes the area-specialists when the typology's basic applicability is refused; Susanoo is the inversion problem's strongest-end exemplar within the four area-specialist refusals registered in this chapter.

=== VI. What inversion teaches

The chapter's five cases — Bida (§I, outcome inversion within a covenant-broken framing), Mwindo (§II, moral-valence inversion within a maturation-through-hubris framing), Sigurd (§III, partial inversion at the long-arc narrative level within an IE-formula reading at the philological level), Pariacaca (§IV, categorical area-specialist refusal in favour of wak'a-succession), and Susanoo (§V, area-specialist refusal of the combat-myth framing itself in favour of metallurgical allegory) — together teach two methodological-typological lessons that the inversion-problem chapter is positioned to consolidate within Part II of the present book.footnote:[Per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml structure §VI on the chapter's two methodological-typological lessons — (a) the combat-myth category has semantic edges that the Bida and Mwindo cases expose sharply; (b) the comparativist pattern of forcing every serpent-fight into the chaoskampf frame is the move that Ballentine, the area-specialists, and scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" argue against; per the chapter's §§I–V on the five cases that supply the evidence for the two lessons.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ballentine-2015 ; loc=on the chaos-order frame's overextension across traditions

The first lesson is that the combat-myth category has *semantic edges* that the Bida and Mwindo cases expose sharply: the chaoskampf typology is robust at its centre (Marduk-Tiamat, Baal-Yamm, Zeus-Typhon, Indra-Vṛtra, Thor-Jörmungandr, Ra-Apep, Teshub-Illuyanka — the cases at which Forsyth 1987 anchors the long-arc combat-myth tradition) but its applicability at its edges is contingent on whether the case's own categorical apparatus is compatible with the typology's chaos-versus-order opposition, and the Bida and Mwindo cases are the present book's clearest evidence that the typology has edges at which the comparativist's standard reading runs against the area-specialists' framings in ways that compromise the comparison's precision unless the area-specialists' framings are presented first.footnote:[Per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml structure §VI on the first lesson; Forsyth 1987, *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth*, on the long-arc combat-myth tradition's centre; per the present chapter's §§I–II on the Bida and Mwindo cases as evidence for the typology's semantic edges; Ballentine 2015 on the typological discipline of recognising the chaoskampf frame's semantic edges.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=forsyth-1987 ; loc=combat-myth long-arc typology (Sumer through Judeo-Christian apocalyptic)

The first lesson, in its precise form, is *not* that the chaoskampf typology fails — the typology remains the present book's principal organising scheme, and Part II's eight comparative chapters are themselves the typology's precise articulation across cosmogony (C1), sovereignty (C2), cyclical maintenance (C3), eschatological displacement (C4), the IE HERO SLAY SERPENT formula (C5), the West-Asia / North-Africa Levantine cluster (C6), the present inversion problem (C7), and the refusal-and-appropriation problem (C8) — but that the typology's *strong* cases are the cases at which the area-specialists' framings and the chaoskampf typology's apparatus *converge*, and the typology's *edges* are the cases at which the convergence is partial, contested, or absent, and the typology's analytic discipline is to register the difference between the two rather than to extend the typology's reach by force.footnote:[Per scope.md §"Subject definition" on the four-subtype operationalisation that Part II's first four comparative chapters articulate; per scope.md §"Book structure" on Part II's eight comparative chapters as the typology's precise articulation; per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml structure §VI on the typology's strong cases as the convergence cases and the typology's edges as the partial-or-contested-or-absent-convergence cases; Ballentine 2015 on the typological discipline of registering the convergence-or-not.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ballentine-2015 ; loc=on the typological discipline of registering convergence-or-not between area-specialist framings and comparative typology

The second lesson is that the comparativist pattern of forcing every serpent-fight into the chaoskampf frame is *itself a category-imposition* that the area-specialists, Ballentine 2015, and scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" all argue against: when the typology's reach is extended to cases whose own categorical apparatus is structurally different (Mwindo's reciprocal-balance moral-cosmography; Pariacaca's wak'a-succession; Susanoo's metallurgical-political charter), the typology's standard reading either inverts (Bida; Mwindo at the moral-valence level) or imports an apparatus the area-specialists do not employ (Pariacaca; Susanoo) or does both at different analytic levels (Sigurd at the long-arc level), and the resulting "inversion" or "deviation" or "exception" classifications are themselves artifacts of the typology's reach being extended beyond the convergence cases.footnote:[Per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml structure §VI on the second lesson; Ballentine 2015, *The Conflict Myth and the Biblical Tradition*, on the comparativist pattern of forcing every serpent-fight into the chaoskampf frame as itself a category-imposition; per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" on the area-specialist citation policy that the present chapter applies as a methodological corrective to the category-imposition pattern.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ballentine-2015 ; loc=on the comparativist pattern of forcing every serpent-fight into the chaoskampf frame as category-imposition

[SPECULATION: the second lesson, taken in its strong form, holds that the comparativist pattern of forcing every serpent-fight into the chaoskampf frame is *itself a category-imposition* that the area-specialists' refusals in §§II, IV, and V all push back against, and that the appropriate methodological response is not to abandon the chaoskampf typology (which would be too strong, since the typology is robust at its convergence cases) but to apply the typology with the area-specialist-first discipline that this chapter demonstrates. | basis: Ballentine 2015's argument that the chaoskampf frame has been overextended in twentieth-century comparative scholarship (in particular her case that the biblical "chaos-versus-order" reading of *tehôm* and Leviathan has been imposed on the Hebrew Bible by Gunkel-tradition comparativism rather than emerging from the Hebrew material's own framing) supplies the methodological warrant; scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" supplies the institutional commitment; the present chapter's §§I–V supply the case-level evidence (Mwindo's full refusal of the chaos-order moral-cosmographic apparatus; Pariacaca's categorical refusal in favour of wak'a-succession; Susanoo's refusal of the combat-myth framing itself in favour of metallurgical allegory). | counterargument: a typological recurrence is still detectable across the corpus — Marduk-Tiamat, Baal-Yamm, Zeus-Typhon, Indra-Vṛtra, Thor-Jörmungandr, Ra-Apep, Teshub-Illuyanka, and (with the qualifications of §§I–III) Bida-Mamadi, Mwindo-Kirimu, and Sigurd-Fáfnir all share enough surface-narrative features (storm-or-warrior-deity, serpentine adversary, defeat by combat or stratagem, recovery of weapon or treasure) that the recurrence cannot be dismissed as comparativist projection alone, and the typology's edges (in this strong-form reading) are evidence for the typology's analytic discipline rather than for its abandonment; the chapter's reading of the second lesson is therefore the chapter's reading rather than the only available scholarly position, and a defender of the typology's wider applicability could read the inversion cases as instances of a more elastic typology rather than as evidence against the typology's category-imposition tendency.]

The two lessons together support the chapter's central methodological commitment, registered in advance in the source overview and demonstrated case-by-case in §§I–V: the *area-specialists speak first*, and the comparison the chapter performs is *more* — not less — precise as a result; the Soninke covenant-frame for Bida (Conrad and Belcher), the Nyanga maturation-through-hubris frame for Mwindo (Biebuyck and Mateene), the Watkins-1995 IE-formula reading and the long-arc tragedy reading for Sigurd (jointly within the IE-comparative tradition), the wak'a-succession frame for Pariacaca (Salomon, Urioste, Taylor, Dean), and the metallurgical-allegory frame for Orochi (Naumann, Isomae, Como, Ooms) are each presented on their own terms before the chapter invokes the chaoskampf category, and the resulting comparison is at the level of *structural observation* rather than at the level of categorical-apparatus imposition.footnote:[Per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" on the priority of area-specialist framings; per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml special_instructions on the chapter's theoretical contribution being that allowing the area-specialists to speak first does not destroy the comparison but makes it more precise; per the present chapter's §§I–V on the case-level demonstrations of the methodological commitment.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ballentine-2015 ; loc=on the methodological discipline of separating area-specialist framings from comparative typology
