The chapter's final methodological observation is that the inversion problem and the appropriation problem (developed at length in C8 / Ch41) are not separate problems but the same problem viewed at different levels of the typology's reach: the inversion problem is what the typology's edges look like *at the level of the cases themselves*, when the comparativist tries to fit the cases into the typology's apparatus and the cases push back; the appropriation problem is what the typology's edges look like *at the level of the comparativist's reading apparatus*, when the comparativist asks what is owed to the area-specialists whose framings have pushed back, and the two problems' shared resolution is the area-specialist-first discipline that this chapter has demonstrated and that the next chapter (C8) consolidates.footnote:[Per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml structure §VI on the inversion problem and the appropriation problem as the same problem viewed at different levels of the typology's reach; per scope.md §"Book structure" Part II on C7 (inversion) and C8 (refusal and appropriation) as the closing two chapters of Part II; per the present chapter's §§I–V on the case-level demonstrations that the next chapter (C8) consolidates at the methodological level.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ballentine-2015 ; loc=on the methodological discipline of integrating area-specialist framings into comparative typology

The chapter therefore closes with the observation registered at the source overview's methodological-commitment paragraph and now demonstrated across the five cases of §§I–V: the chaoskampf typology is the present book's principal organising scheme, the typology is robust at its convergence cases (Part II's first four comparative chapters anchor those cases), the typology has edges at its non-convergence cases (the present chapter and Ch41 articulate those edges), and the typology's analytic discipline is to register the convergence-or-not at the level of the area-specialists' framings rather than to extend the typology's reach by force — and the inversion problem is, on the chapter's reading, not a refutation of the typology but the typology's most rigorous test, a test the typology passes only when the area-specialists are allowed to speak first.footnote:[Per scope.md §"Subject definition" on the chaoskampf typology as the present book's principal organising scheme; per scope.md §"Book structure" on Part II's eight comparative chapters as the typology's articulation; per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml comparative_argument and special_instructions on the chapter's theoretical contribution; Ballentine 2015 on the typological discipline applied at the chapter's closing observation.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ballentine-2015 ; loc=on the typological discipline of registering convergence-or-not at the area-specialist-framing level

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C1. Combat as cosmogony (Ch34) — the §VI observation that the typology is robust at its convergence cases and has edges at its non-convergence cases is registered against the C1 anchor (Marduk-Tiamat) as the typology's strong-cosmogonic convergence case; the inversion-problem cases of §§I–V of the present chapter are the typology's non-convergence cases against which the C1 anchor's convergence is contrasted.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C2. Combat as sovereignty (Ch35) — the §VI observation is similarly registered against the C2 anchors (Baal-Yamm, Zeus-Typhon) as the typology's strong-sovereignty convergence cases; the inversion-problem cases are the typology's non-convergence cases against which the C2 anchors' convergence is contrasted.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C5. The IE HERO SLAY SERPENT formula (Ch38) — the §III treatment of Sigurd as the partial case at which the IE-formula reading and the long-arc tragedy of the Sigurd-cycle pull in different directions is the chapter's IE-internal complement to the area-specialist-objection cases of §§I–II and §§IV–V; the C5 chapter's IE-comparative reading of the formula is the structural-comparative foil for §III.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C8. Refusal and appropriation (Ch41) — the §VI observation that the inversion problem and the appropriation problem are the same problem viewed at different levels of the typology's reach prepares the C8 chapter's meta-question of what comparativism owes the area-specialists; the inversion-problem chapter is the case-level demonstration that the appropriation-problem chapter consolidates at the methodological level.

# Input file: chapters/40-C7-inversion-problem.adoc

[[ch-40-C7-inversion-problem]]
== C7. The Inversion Problem

=== Source overview

This chapter is the seventh of the eight Part-II comparative chapters and is positioned as the comparative chapter in which the chaoskampf typology's *edges* are tested most rigorously: the cases gathered in §§I–V are the cases at which the standard chaoskampf description either runs in reverse (the slaying of the serpent ends rather than establishes prosperity), is morally inverted (the slaying is hubris and is punished), or is held by area-specialists not to be a chaoskampf reading at all (the _wak'a_-succession reading of the Pariacaca cycle; the metallurgical-allegory reading of the Susanoo / Yamata no Orochi episode).footnote:[Per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml comparative_argument on the inversion problem as the chapter's organising question; per scope.md §"Subject definition" on the typology's four-subtype operationalisation that Part II tests; per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" on the area-specialist citation requirement that the present chapter applies more rigorously than any other Part-II comparative chapter.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ballentine-2015 ; loc=on the chaos-order frame's elastic application across traditions

The chapter draws on five Part-I chapters of the present book — Ch28 (Bida and the founding of Wagadu), Ch29 (Mwindo and Kirimu), Ch19 §"Sigurd vs. Fáfnir" within the IE-reflexes chapter, Ch26 (Pariacaca and the _amaru_), and Ch23 (Susanoo vs. Yamata no Orochi) — and the per-tradition factual claims in §§I–V are routed through the same `sources.yaml` IDs that the Part-I chapters use for those facts: conrad-west-africa and belcher-epic for the Bida material, biebuyck-mateene-mwindo and belcher-epic for the Mwindo material, watkins-1995 and larrington-poetic-edda for the Sigurd material, salomon-urioste-huarochiri for the Pariacaca material, and naumann-mythen and philippi-kojiki for the Susanoo material.footnote:[Per scope.md §"Comparative-chapter discipline" on the upstream-lookup rule that routes per-tradition factual claims in comparative chapters through the same sources.yaml IDs the upstream Part-I chapters use; per chapters/28-bida-wagadu.claims.approved.adoc, chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc, chapters/19-ie-reflexes.claims.approved.adoc, chapters/26-pariacaca-amaru.claims.approved.adoc, and chapters/23-susanoo-orochi.claims.approved.adoc on the per-tradition source-IDs that the present chapter inherits.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The chapter's principal anchors per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml `sources.anchor` are six: Conrad 2005 (*Empires of Medieval West Africa*) on Bida and Wagadu; Belcher 1999 (*Epic Traditions of Africa*) on Bida and on Mwindo; Biebuyck and Mateene 1969 (*The Mwindo Epic from the Banyanga (Zaire)*) as the primary text for Mwindo; Watkins 1995 (*How to Kill a Dragon: Aspects of Indo-European Poetics*), ch. 42, on the Sigurd ambiguity; Naumann 1996 (*Die Mythen des alten Japan*) on Susanoo / Orochi as metallurgical allegory; and Salomon and Urioste 1991 (*The Huarochirí Manuscript*) on Pariacaca and the _amaru_ as _wak'a_-succession.footnote:[Per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml sources.anchor on the six anchor entries: Conrad 2005, Belcher 1999, Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, Watkins 1995, Naumann 1996, Salomon & Urioste 1991; comparative chapters use sources.anchor in place of sources.primary per the established Part-II chapter-claims pattern.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 42

A further set of named area-specialists supplies the area-specialist framings on which §§I–V depend but is not currently in `sources.yaml`: David Conrad and Stephen Belcher are the named anchors for the Soninke / Mande oral-tradition reading of the Bida narrative (both in `sources.yaml`); Daniel Biebuyck and Kahombo Mateene are the named anchors for the Nyanga maturation-through-hubris reading of the Mwindo epic (in `sources.yaml`); Frank Salomon, George Urioste, Gérald Taylor, and Carolyn Dean are the named area-specialists for the Andean _wak'a_-succession reading (Salomon and Urioste in `sources.yaml`; Taylor and Dean named-but-flagged in body prose per the named-but-flagged secondary-anchor pattern of the Part-I chapters); Nelly Naumann, Jun'ichi Isomae, Michael Como, and Herman Ooms are the named area-specialists for the Japanese metallurgical-allegory reading (Naumann in `sources.yaml`; Isomae, Como, and Ooms named-but-flagged in body prose per the same pattern), and the chapter cites the latter three by name in body prose where their framing is load-bearing for the area-specialist reading without routing any EVIDENCE token through them.footnote:[Per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" on the area-specialist citation requirement that the chapter must cite Conrad, Belcher, Biebuyck and Mateene, Salomon, Taylor, Naumann, Isomae, Como, and Ooms by name; per the named-but-flagged secondary-anchor pattern of the Part-I chapters (Ch26 for Taylor and Dean; Ch23 for Isomae, Como, and Ooms) on the citation discipline applied here. Taylor's Lima IFEA critical edition of the *Huarochirí Manuscript* is named in body prose at §IV per the same pattern; Isomae, Como, and Ooms are named in body prose at §V per the same pattern; the present chapter routes no EVIDENCE token through any of these named-but-flagged scholars and the named-but-flagged uses are documented in the chapter's completion record for inventory-audit follow-up.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=salomon-urioste-huarochiri ; loc=Huarochirí Manuscript translators' introduction
// EVIDENCE: source_id=naumann-mythen ; loc=Orochi chapter

The chapter's general comparative anchor for the long-arc combat-myth tradition against which the inversion cases are read is Neil Forsyth's *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth* (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 1987), whose Sumer-through-Judeo-Christian-apocalyptic survey supplies the long-arc combat-myth typology that the present chapter takes as one starting point and that Bida, Mwindo, the Sigurd-cycle long arc, the Pariacaca cycle, and the Susanoo / Orochi episode all complicate in different ways; the chapter's principal critical-typological anchor for the meta-claim that the chaoskampf category is itself elastic-to-the-point-of-imposition is Debra Scurlock Ballentine's *The Conflict Myth and the Biblical Tradition* (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2015), whose argument that the chaoskampf frame has been overextended in twentieth-century comparative scholarship supplies the chapter's §VI methodological warrant.footnote:[Forsyth 1987, *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth* (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 1987; ISBN 9780691014746), as the long-arc combat-myth typology anchor against which the present chapter's inversion cases are read; Ballentine 2015, *The Conflict Myth and the Biblical Tradition* (Oxford: Oxford University Press, 2015; ISBN 9780190253028), on the over-extension critique of the chaoskampf frame in twentieth-century comparative scholarship as the chapter's §VI methodological warrant.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=forsyth-1987 ; loc=combat-myth long-arc typology (Sumer through Judeo-Christian apocalyptic)
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ballentine-2015 ; loc=on the chaos-order frame's overextension across traditions

The chapter's evidentiary discipline follows the comparative-chapter pattern established at Chs 33–38: every per-tradition factual claim is routed through the same `sources.yaml` ID that the corresponding Part-I chapter uses for that fact; doubled EVIDENCE tokens are reserved for the points at which the chapter explicitly bridges an area-specialist anchor and a comparative-typology anchor (most notably in §III, where Watkins reads Sigurd's killing of Fáfnir through the IE formula but the Eddic poet, on Larrington's translation, presents the dialogue as a wisdom-exchange and a curse-warning); §§I, II, IV, and V are mostly single-token because the chapter's argument runs through the area-specialists' own framings rather than through cross-cultural transmission.footnote:[Per the chapter-claims skill instructions on the upstream-lookup rule and the no-invented-source-IDs rule; per the doubled-token discipline established at Ch34 (C1) and developed at Chs 35–38 on the single-token default with doubled-token bridges where the chapter explicitly contrasts a per-tradition area-specialist anchor with a comparative-typology anchor.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ballentine-2015 ; loc=on the methodological discipline of separating area-specialist framings from comparative typology

The chapter's structure follows the brief's six-section outline: §I treats Bida (Soninke / Wagadu) as the anchor case for the inversion problem, presenting the narrative's covenant-broken framing on the Soninke tradition's own terms before invoking the modern comparativist "inverse chaoskampf" overlay; §II treats Mwindo (Nyanga) as the full refusal, presenting the maturation-through-hubris framing on the Nyanga tradition's own terms before noting that the moral valence of the dragon-killing is inverted from chaoskampf rather than the outcome; §III treats Sigurd (Old Norse Eddic Sigurd-cycle) as the partial case in which Watkins's IE-formula reading and the long-arc tragedy of the cycle pull in different directions; §IV treats Pariacaca (early-colonial Andean *Huarochirí Manuscript*) as an area-specialist refusal of the chaos-order framing in favour of _wak'a_-succession; §V treats Susanoo / Yamata no Orochi (early-Japanese imperial chronicles) as an area-specialist refusal in favour of metallurgical allegory and sword-etiology; §VI draws the methodological-typological lessons.footnote:[Per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml structure on the six-section outline (I. Bida; II. Mwindo; III. Sigurd; IV. Pariacaca; V. Susanoo; VI. What inversion teaches); per brief special_instructions on Bida and Mwindo as main weight and on the area-specialist-first discipline applied at §§I–II.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

The chapter's central methodological commitment, registered in advance and demonstrated case-by-case in §§I–V, is that the *area-specialists speak first*: the Soninke covenant-frame for Bida (Conrad and Belcher), the Nyanga maturation-through-hubris frame for Mwindo (Biebuyck and Mateene), the _wak'a_-succession frame for Pariacaca (Salomon, Urioste, Taylor, Dean), and the metallurgical-allegory frame for Orochi (Naumann, Isomae, Como, Ooms) are presented on their own terms before the chapter invokes the chaoskampf category, and the chapter takes the resulting comparison to be more — not less — precise as a result; the methodological warrant is scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" on the priority of area-specialist framings, and the demonstration of the warrant is the chapter's principal contribution to the book's typological discipline.footnote:[Per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" on the priority of area-specialist framings; per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml special_instructions on the chapter's theoretical contribution being that the "inverse chaoskampf" reading is itself a comparativist artifact and that allowing the area-specialists to speak first does not destroy the comparison but makes it more precise; Ballentine 2015 on the typological discipline of separating area-specialist framings from comparative-typology overlays.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ballentine-2015 ; loc=on the typological discipline of separating area-specialist framings from comparative typology

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C1. Combat as cosmogony (Ch34) — the once-and-done structure of Marduk's cosmogonic combat with Tiamat, taken as the chaoskampf typology's strong-cosmogonic anchor in Ch34, is contrasted in the present chapter §I with the covenant-broken-and-prosperity-ended structure of the Bida / Wagadu narrative; the inversion is at the level of outcome rather than at the level of structure.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C8. Refusal and appropriation (Ch41) — the present chapter prepares the appropriation-problem chapter by demonstrating the area-specialist-first discipline at five cases of varying severity (full refusal: Mwindo; refusal of the chaos-order framing in favour of wak'a-succession: Pariacaca; refusal of the combat-myth framing in favour of metallurgical allegory: Susanoo; partial: Sigurd; modern overlay on a covenant-broken story: Bida), and Ch41 develops the meta-question of what comparativism owes the area-specialists when the typology's own categories are contested.

What follows is organised in six body sections. Section I treats Bida (Soninke / Wagadu) as the chapter's anchor case, presenting the covenant-broken framing on the Soninke tradition's own terms before invoking the modern comparativist "inverse chaoskampf" overlay; section II treats Mwindo (Nyanga) as the full refusal, presenting the maturation-through-hubris framing on the Nyanga tradition's own terms before noting the moral-valence inversion; section III treats Sigurd (Old Norse Eddic Sigurd-cycle) as the partial case in which Watkins's IE-formula reading and the long-arc tragedy of the cycle pull in different directions; section IV treats Pariacaca (early-colonial Andean *Huarochirí Manuscript*) as an area-specialist refusal of the chaos-order framing in favour of _wak'a_-succession; section V treats Susanoo / Yamata no Orochi (early-Japanese imperial chronicles) as an area-specialist refusal in favour of metallurgical allegory and sword-etiology; section VI draws the methodological-typological lessons.

=== I. The anchor case — Bida and the covenant-broken framing of the Soninke Wagadu narrative

The Bida narrative is the chapter's anchor case for the inversion problem because the Soninke tradition's covenant-broken framing is best developed in the current English-language scholarship and because the inversion the comparativist sees runs at the level of outcome rather than at the level of the narrative's own categories.

The Soninke / Mande oral-tradition narrative of Bida and Wagadu, recorded across the late-nineteenth- and twentieth-century European-language ethnographic corpus and synthesised in current English-language scholarship by David Conrad's *Empires of Medieval West Africa: Ghana, Mali, and Songhay* (Facts on File, 2005) and Stephen Belcher's *Epic Traditions of Africa* (Indiana University Press, 1999), tells of the seven-headed serpent Bida who is the protector-spirit of the city of Wagadu — the historical western-Sahelian polity known in the Arabic-language chronicle tradition as Ghana, distinct from the modern nation-state of the same name — and to whom the city's prosperity is bound by an annual covenant whose principal stipulation is the delivery of a young woman as tribute, with the city's continued flourishing contingent on the maintenance of the covenant.footnote:[Per chapters/28-bida-wagadu.claims.approved.adoc on the seven-headed serpent Bida as the protector-spirit of Wagadu, on the annual maiden-tribute as the covenant's principal stipulation, and on the covenant's load-bearing function for the city's prosperity; Conrad 2005, *Empires of Medieval West Africa*, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on Wagadu as the medieval western-Sahelian polity (eighth through eleventh-to-twelfth century CE) known in the Arabic-language chronicle tradition as Ghana; Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Soninke traditions, on the narrative's place in the Soninke / Mande oral-epic tradition.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

The covenant's narrative-structural role within the Soninke tradition's own framing is the load-bearing organising principle of the entire narrative arc, not a peripheral motif: the city of Wagadu exists *because* of the covenant with Bida, the city's prosperity *consists in* the rains that fall and the gold that is dug, and the city's eventual abandonment is presented as the *consequence* of breaking the covenant — the narrative's cosmography is the already-existing Sahelian world of Soninke ethnogeographic memory rather than a cosmogonically-emerging cosmos, and the narrative's own logic is contractual-and-aetiological rather than cosmogonic.footnote:[Per chapters/28-bida-wagadu.claims.approved.adoc on the Soninke oral-tradition's framing of the Wagadu narrative as foundational and aetiological, on the covenant as the narrative's load-bearing organising principle, and on the cosmographic situation of the narrative within the already-existing Sahelian world rather than within a cosmogonic emergence; Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Soninke traditions, on the connected-foundation-story character of the narrative; Conrad 2005, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on the contractual-aetiological logic of the narrative's covenant-and-consequence structure.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

The covenant's principal stipulation — the annual delivery of a young Soninke woman to Bida — is not a free-floating dragon-feeding motif but is bound up with the city's specifically-named institutions: the rulers of Wagadu maintain the covenant on the city's behalf, the population of the city contributes to its ritual maintenance, and the maiden delivered each year is identified by name and lineage in the recorded versions, with her selection (and her family's standing) participating in the city's social-political fabric rather than being an anonymous "victim of the dragon" in the European chivalric-romance sense.footnote:[Per chapters/28-bida-wagadu.claims.approved.adoc on the social-political-institutional embedding of the maiden-tribute covenant within Wagadu's named institutions — the rulers of the city as covenant-maintainers, the population as ritual-contributors, and the maiden delivered each year as an individually-named figure with stated lineage in the recorded versions; Conrad 2005, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on the institutional embedding of the covenant.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

The covenant's breaking is precipitated by the suitor Mamadi Sefe Dekote — a figure whose name varies somewhat across the recorded versions (Mamadi Sefe Dekote being the standard form per current Soninke-tradition scholarship in Belcher 1999 and Conrad 2005) — when his beloved is selected as the year's tribute and he, refusing to allow her to be delivered to Bida, ambushes the serpent at the moment of the annual offering and kills him; the narrative's recorded versions specify the manner of the killing (decapitation, with the seven heads severed in succession) and the location (the place of the annual offering, in the city's ritual landscape) with sufficient consistency across the recordings that the killing is securely the narrative's central act, and the narrative arc that follows the killing is the narrative's distinctive feature — not the killing itself, but its consequences.footnote:[Per chapters/28-bida-wagadu.claims.approved.adoc on Mamadi Sefe Dekote as the suitor whose intervention precipitates the covenant-breaking, on the killing's manner (decapitation of the seven heads in succession) and location (the place of the annual offering in the city's ritual landscape), and on the killing's consistency across the recorded versions; Belcher 1999, ch. on Soninke traditions, on the killing's narrative-structural centrality and on the consequences-of-the-killing as the distinctive feature of the narrative arc; per glossary 'Mamadi Sefe Dekote' first_mention_gloss on the suitor's role in the narrative.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

The consequences of the killing, in the recorded versions of the Soninke narrative, are catastrophic and explicitly framed as the covenant's broken-state coming due: the rains stop, the springs dry, the gold ceases to be found, and the population of Wagadu disperses across the western Sahel — with the dispersal supplying the narrative's foundation-charter for the Soninke diaspora that current Soninke communities trace to the abandonment of the polity, and with the dryland Sahelian landscape of present-day Mauritania, western Mali, and adjacent regions being read in the tradition as the visible aftermath of the covenant's breaking.footnote:[Per chapters/28-bida-wagadu.claims.approved.adoc on the catastrophic consequences of the covenant-breaking — the cessation of the rains, the drying of the springs, the cessation of the gold, the dispersal of the Soninke population across the western Sahel — and on the consequences' narrative-structural function as the foundation-charter for the Soninke diaspora and as the aetiology of the dryland Sahelian landscape; Conrad 2005, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on the same catastrophic-consequences pattern; Belcher 1999, ch. on Soninke traditions, on the foundation-charter and aetiological functions of the consequences-narrative.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

The narrative's *internal* moral evaluation of the covenant-breaking is, on the evidence of the recorded versions and as Belcher 1999 and Conrad 2005 read them, neither univocally condemnatory of Mamadi Sefe Dekote nor univocally exculpatory: the suitor acts to save his beloved and the narrative grants him the legitimacy of the rescuing-suitor role, but the narrative also registers the destruction of Wagadu as the consequence of his act, with the result that the Soninke tradition's framing presents the dilemma at the heart of the narrative — the conflict between an individual's claim on his beloved and the collective covenant on which the city's prosperity rests — without resolving the dilemma in favour of either claim, and the narrative's didactic register is the registration of the dilemma rather than its adjudication.footnote:[Per chapters/28-bida-wagadu.claims.approved.adoc on the internal moral evaluation of the covenant-breaking in the Soninke tradition's framing — neither univocally condemnatory of Mamadi Sefe Dekote nor univocally exculpatory, with the conflict between the individual's claim and the collective covenant the narrative's load-bearing dilemma; Belcher 1999, ch. on Soninke traditions, on the dilemma-registering rather than dilemma-adjudicating function of the recorded narrative; Conrad 2005, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend, on the same moral-ambivalence pattern in the recorded versions.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

The covenant-broken framing has no need of the chaoskampf category to make sense of itself: the narrative's protagonists, motivations, and consequences are exhausted within the covenant-and-consequence frame, and a hearer who reads the narrative within the Soninke tradition's own moral and aetiological registers does not require any concept of "primordial chaos" or "cosmos-establishing combat" to follow what happens or to take the moral point — the narrative is *complete* within its own categories, and the chaoskampf reading of it is an *additional* analytic move performed from outside the Soninke tradition by a reader whose reading apparatus has been formed elsewhere.footnote:[Per chapters/28-bida-wagadu.claims.approved.adoc on the completeness of the covenant-broken framing within the Soninke tradition's own categories and on the chaoskampf reading as an additional analytic move performed from outside the tradition; Belcher 1999, ch. on Soninke traditions, on the self-sufficiency of the Soninke tradition's framing for the narrative; Ballentine 2015, *The Conflict Myth and the Biblical Tradition*, on the methodological discipline of separating area-specialist framings from comparative-typology overlays.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ballentine-2015 ; loc=on the methodological discipline of separating area-specialist framings from comparative typology

Once the area-specialists' framing is in place, the comparativist's "inverse chaoskampf" reading of the narrative can be registered without subordinating the area-specialists' framing to it: read against the long-arc combat-myth typology that Forsyth 1987 surveys (Sumer through Judeo-Christian apocalyptic), the Bida narrative *does* exhibit a striking inversion of the standard typological outcome — the slaying of the serpent does not establish prosperity, it ends it; the serpent is not the cosmos's adversary, it is the city's protector; and the killing does not deliver order, it delivers the dryland Sahel — and the inversion is structurally legible as soon as the typology is brought to bear, even though the inversion itself is not what the Soninke tradition is *doing* with the narrative on its own terms.footnote:[Per chapters/28-bida-wagadu.claims.approved.adoc on the inversion-of-typology reading as comparativist overlay rather than as Soninke tradition's own framing; Forsyth 1987, *The Old Enemy*, on the long-arc combat-myth typology against which the inversion is structurally legible; per brief 28-bida-wagadu.yaml special_instructions on the discipline of presenting the Soninke framing first and only then registering the inversion against the comparative typology.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=forsyth-1987 ; loc=combat-myth long-arc typology (Sumer through Judeo-Christian apocalyptic)

The "inverse chaoskampf" framing is therefore a real comparative observation but also a *modern overlay*: the Soninke griot tradition that transmits the narrative does not need the chaoskampf category to recite or to interpret it, the audiences who hear the narrative within Soninke communities do not encounter it as the inverse of any other narrative they know, and the inversion-reading exists at the level of the typological synthesis that the comparativist performs from the outside — a real reading, but one whose epistemic standing is comparativist-typological rather than tradition-internal.footnote:[Per chapters/28-bida-wagadu.claims.approved.adoc on the inverse-chaoskampf framing as a modern overlay performed at the level of the typological synthesis rather than at the level of the Soninke tradition's own self-understanding; Belcher 1999, ch. on Soninke traditions, on the tradition-internal interpretive resources of the recorded versions; per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" on the methodological status of comparativist-typological readings as additional moves rather than as primary readings of non-Eurasian materials.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

[APPROPRIATION-FLAG: the area-specialist objection registered here is that the Bida / Wagadu narrative's *own* framing within the Soninke / Mande oral tradition is covenant-broken — a contractual-and-aetiological framing in which the city's prosperity rests on a maintained covenant with the protector-serpent and ends with the covenant's breaking — and not "inverse chaoskampf", a frame imported from the comparative typology of Eurasian combat-myth scholarship and applied to the Soninke material from outside; the objection is not that the chaoskampf typology fails to capture *anything* about the narrative (the narrative does include a serpent-killing, and the inversion-of-outcome is structurally legible against the typology) but that the typology mis-locates the narrative's load-bearing categories: where chaoskampf places "cosmos versus chaos" and "order-establishment by combat" at the centre, the Soninke framing places "covenant versus covenant-breaking" and "consequence-of-breaking-the-covenant" at the centre, and the two frames are structurally different rather than mirror-image. | named_specialists: David Conrad (*Empires of Medieval West Africa*, 2005, ch. on the Ghana / Wagadu legend; in sources.yaml as conrad-west-africa) and Stephen Belcher (*Epic Traditions of Africa*, 1999, ch. on Soninke traditions; in sources.yaml as belcher-epic) are the principal English-language area-specialist anchors for the covenant-broken reading; the wider Mande-area area-specialist tradition associated with Charles Monteil's "La légende du Ouagadou" (1953 IFAN-Dakar; named in body prose at Ch28 per the named-but-flagged secondary-anchor pattern) and with Germaine Dieterlen and the École française de l'Afrique de l'Ouest (named in body prose at Ch28 per the same pattern) supplies the wider area-specialist scholarly tradition within which Conrad and Belcher's English-language synthesis is set. | tier_implication: scope.md classifies the Bida / Wagadu narrative as RELATED-tier (full, inversion anchor) rather than CORE-tier precisely because the area-specialist framing is covenant-broken rather than chaoskampf-inverted, and the chaoskampf reading is registered as a comparativist overlay rather than as a tradition-internal feature; the tier-classification is itself a recognition of the area-specialists' priority. | forwards_to: this APPROPRIATION-FLAG is engaged at length in the present comparative chapter (Ch40, the inversion problem) and again in Ch41 (the refusal-and-appropriation problem), where the meta-question of what comparativism owes the area-specialists when the typology's own categories are contested is the chapter's organising question.]

The methodological pay-off of presenting the covenant-broken framing first, before invoking the inverse-chaoskampf overlay, is that the comparison the chapter performs is *more* precise rather than less: the chaoskampf typology is not asked to do work the narrative resists, the area-specialists' framing is not subordinated to a comparative apparatus that does not fit it, and the comparison between the Bida narrative and the Eurasian combat-myth corpus can proceed at the level of *structural inversion of one feature* (the slaying ends prosperity rather than establishing it) without forcing the comparison through a categorical apparatus (cosmos versus chaos) that the Soninke material does not employ.footnote:[Per chapters/28-bida-wagadu.claims.approved.adoc on the methodological pay-off of presenting the covenant-broken framing first, on the structural-inversion-of-one-feature reading as the precise comparative observation that the chapter delivers, and on the resulting precision of the comparison relative to the categorical-apparatus-imposing reading; Ballentine 2015, on the methodological discipline of separating area-specialist framings from comparative-typology overlays as the route to more precise comparison.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ballentine-2015 ; loc=on the methodological discipline of separating area-specialist framings from comparative typology

The Bida narrative's textual situation reinforces the methodological point: the narrative is preserved entirely in late-nineteenth- and twentieth-century European-language ethnographic recordings of Soninke griots and tradition-bearers (Frobenius's *Atlantis* IV–VI from 1921 onward; Monteil's "La légende du Ouagadou" of 1953 in IFAN-Dakar; the École française de l'Afrique de l'Ouest tradition associated with Germaine Dieterlen from the 1940s onward), with no pre-modern manuscript attestation and with the post-colonial-anthropology filtering caveat that the Frobenius and Monteil strands of the recording corpus require — and the recording-history situation is itself a reason to take the Soninke tradition's *own* framing seriously rather than assimilating the narrative to a comparative typology developed for materials with quite different textual situations.footnote:[Per chapters/28-bida-wagadu.claims.approved.adoc on the recording-history situation of the Bida narrative — Frobenius's *Atlantis* IV–VI from 1921 onward, Monteil's "La légende du Ouagadou" of 1953 in IFAN-Dakar, and the École française de l'Afrique de l'Ouest tradition associated with Germaine Dieterlen from the 1940s onward — and on the post-colonial-anthropology filtering caveat that the Frobenius and Monteil strands require; Belcher 1999, ch. on Soninke traditions, on the recording-history as a reason to take the Soninke tradition's own framing seriously.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Soninke traditions

The comparative observation the chapter takes from §I, registered in advance of the Mwindo case in §II, is that the Bida narrative's *outcome* is inverted relative to the Eurasian combat-myth typology (the slaying ends rather than establishes prosperity), and the *internal moral evaluation* of the inversion within the Soninke tradition is the dilemma-registration that Belcher 1999 and Conrad 2005 both observe — neither vindicating the slayer nor exonerating the city's covenant-maintainers — and that the comparison's interesting feature is not the inversion per se but the area-specialist framing that makes the inversion legible *as a structural observation* rather than as a moral or theological claim about either tradition.footnote:[Per chapters/28-bida-wagadu.claims.approved.adoc on the inverted-outcome observation as the comparative chapter's principal load-bearing finding for §I; Belcher 1999 and Conrad 2005 on the dilemma-registering moral evaluation of the killing within the Soninke tradition; per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml structure on the chapter's framing of the inversion as a structural observation rather than as a moral or theological claim.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=conrad-west-africa ; loc=Empires of Medieval West Africa, ch. on Ghana/Wagadu legend

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C1. Combat as cosmogony (Ch34) — the once-and-done order-establishing structure of Marduk's combat with Tiamat in *Enūma Eliš*, taken in Ch34 as the chaoskampf typology's strong-cosmogonic anchor, is the structural-comparative foil against which the Bida narrative's covenant-broken-and-prosperity-ended structure is read in §I above as inverted *at the level of outcome* (slaying ends rather than establishes prosperity) without being inverted at the level of the underlying categorical apparatus (the Soninke framing runs on covenant-and-consequence rather than on cosmos-versus-chaos).
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C2. Combat as sovereignty (Ch35) — the sovereignty-establishing structure of Baal's combat with Yamm and Zeus's combat with Typhon, taken in Ch35 as the typology's strong-sovereignty anchor, is contrasted in §I above with the sovereignty-ending structure of the Bida slaying: where Baal's and Zeus's slayings establish or secure divine sovereignty, Bida's slaying ends the sovereignty-of-Wagadu that the covenant supported.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C8. Refusal and appropriation (Ch41) — the area-specialist-first discipline applied at §I above, demonstrating how the covenant-broken framing precedes any inverse-chaoskampf overlay, prepares the C8 chapter's meta-question of what comparativism owes the area-specialists when the typology's categories are contested; Bida is the inversion problem's anchor case and the appropriation problem's milder-end exemplar.

=== II. The full refusal — Mwindo and the Nyanga maturation-through-hubris framing

The Mwindo / Kirimu episode is the chapter's most pointed area-specialist refusal of the chaoskampf typology's moral-cosmographic apparatus, and the section presents the Nyanga tradition's maturation-through-hubris framing on its own terms before noting that the moral valence of the dragon-killing is the precise contrary of the typology's standard valuation.

The Nyanga oral epic of Mwindo, recorded in 1956 from the named narrator Shé-Kárisi Candi Rureke at Bese on the upper Oso river in the Kivu region of what was then the eastern Belgian Congo and published in Daniel Biebuyck and Kahombo C. Mateene's *The Mwindo Epic from the Banyanga (Zaire)* (University of California Press, 1969) as the standard English-language critical edition and translation, presents the dragon-slaying not as the establishing act of cosmic order or of the hero's sovereignty but as an act of hubristic over-reach for which the sky-god Nkuba punishes the hero — a structural and moral feature of the recorded performance that Biebuyck and Mateene 1969 and Belcher 1999 (*Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Mwindo) both develop as the load-bearing didactic register of the Kirimu episode within the wider epic.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the 1956 Bese recording from Shé-Kárisi Candi Rureke as the chapter's anchor text and on Biebuyck and Mateene 1969 as the standard English-language critical edition and translation; on the dragon-slaying's narrative-structural function as hubristic over-reach that the sky-god Nkuba punishes; Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa*, ch. on Mwindo, on the maturation-through-hubris reading as the area-specialist consensus reading of the episode's didactic register; per glossary 'Mwindo', 'Kirimu', 'Nkuba' first_mention_glosses on the principal protagonists.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The Nyanga tradition's own framing of the Mwindo epic — as Biebuyck and Mateene 1969 develop it from the recorded performance and as Belcher 1999 reads it within the comparative African-oral-epic apparatus — is a connected biographical-and-moral narrative of the hero's life, not a cosmogonic narrative: the cosmos is already in place when the epic begins, layered into Nkuba's sky-realm above, Muisa's underworld below, and Iyangura's river-and-water realm laterally; Mwindo's miraculous birth, his disputes with his father Shemwindo (chief of Tubondo, hostile to the son's birth), his journeys across the human and spirit worlds, his testing by the powers above and below, and his eventual chastened return as a properly-formed chief organise the epic from beginning to end, and the hero's biographical-and-moral arc is the compositional centre rather than the establishment of any cosmographic frame.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the Nyanga tradition's framing of the *Mwindo Epic* as a connected biographical-and-moral narrative rather than a cosmogonic narrative, on the layered cosmography (Nkuba's sky-realm above, Muisa's underworld below, Iyangura's river-realm laterally) within which the hero's journeys unfold, and on the hero's biographical-and-moral arc as the compositional centre; Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, translators' introduction on the same biographical-arc framing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The middle-epic passages of the recorded performance present Mwindo's accumulating power as *increasingly dangerous*: the hero's aunts and various other figures along the way warn him repeatedly that his power is exceeding what is permitted, the formulaic refrains and direct narrative comments register the powers above and below as watching the hero's career with a view to the eventual moment at which the correction will fall, and the audience is prepared by these passages to read the Kirimu episode when it arrives as the moment for which the foreshadowing pattern has been preparing — not as a triumph but as an act for which the hero will be answerable.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the middle-epic foreshadowing pattern in the recorded performance — formulaic refrains and direct narrative comments registering Mwindo's accumulating power as dangerous, his aunts' warnings, and the announced expectation that the hubris will require correction; on the audience-preparation function of the foreshadowing for the Kirimu episode; Belcher 1999, ch. on Mwindo, on the foreshadowing-of-correction pattern as load-bearing for the epic's middle sections.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

Kirimu — the dragon at the chapter's narrative centre — is presented in the recorded performance as a being of the spirit world whose existence is part of the cosmographic equilibrium between the human and spirit realms, not as a cosmic adversary whose defeat is the cosmos's establishment; the dragon's narrative-structural role is to be the cosmographic counterpart whose presence the human world must respect, and the hero's killing of him is registered in the recorded performance — by formulaic refrains and direct narrative comments — as an *unsanctioned act of human power against the spirit world* rather than as an act of cosmos-ordering.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on Kirimu's narrative-structural role as a being of the spirit world whose existence is part of the cosmographic equilibrium, on the killing's registration in the recorded performance as an unsanctioned act of human power against the spirit world, and on the moral-cosmographic principle (developed in the recorded text's formulaic refrains and direct narrative comments) that such an act demands a counter-act from the powers above to restore the equilibrium; Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, Kirimu episode passages on the same transgression-not-triumph framing.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The killing itself, in the recorded performance, is completed without any of the order-establishing apparatus that the chaoskampf typology expects at this point: there is no narrative passage in which the killing of the dragon establishes the hero's sovereignty, no passage in which the cosmos is reorganised around the dragon's defeat, no festival celebrating the killing as cosmic-order-establishing — the hero kills the dragon, takes the carcass or pieces of it back toward the human settlements, and the narrative immediately turns to Nkuba's response-passage; the *structural absence* of order-establishing apparatus is itself one of the recorded performance's load-bearing features for the Nyanga tradition's reading of the episode.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the killing's completion with no immediate triumphal conclusion — no sovereignty-establishment passage, no cosmos-reorganisation passage, no order-establishing festival — and on the structural absence of order-establishing apparatus as itself a load-bearing feature of the recorded performance; on the immediate narrative turn from the killing to Nkuba's response.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

Nkuba's response is immediate, deliberate, and morally-motivated: the sky-god descends in lightning, seizes the hero out of the human world, and conveys him by lightning from the forest-and-village landscape upward into the celestial realm, where Mwindo is subjected to a sequence of trials and humiliations — exposure to extreme heat and cold, compulsion to endure the celestial powers' direct presence in a way that strips him of his heroic confidence, and explicit instruction about the limits of his power and the necessity of recognising the cosmographic authority of the powers above — that the recorded performance presents not as arbitrary cruelty but as a structurally-necessary correction of the hero's hubristic over-reach.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on Nkuba's lightning-descent and seizing of the hero, on the celestial-realm trials and humiliations (exposure to extreme heat and cold, compulsion to endure the celestial powers' direct presence, explicit instruction about the limits of the hero's power), and on the moral-didactic register of the punishment as a structurally-necessary correction rather than as arbitrary cruelty; per glossary 'Nkuba' first_mention_gloss on Nkuba as Nyanga sky-god who punishes Mwindo for the killing of Kirimu.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The conditions Nkuba imposes on Mwindo's return to the human world are explicit, named, and binding within the recorded performance's framing: the hero is required to renounce certain practices, to accept the binding force of the cosmographic limits the celestial powers have set, and to commit to a chastened mode of conduct as a properly-formed chief on his return — and the hero's eventual restoration to the forest-and-village landscape is conditional and supervised, registered in the recorded performance as a return on the celestial powers' terms rather than as a triumphant homecoming on the hero's own terms.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the conditions Nkuba imposes on Mwindo's return — explicit, named, and binding renunciations and commitments — and on the hero's restoration to the human world as conditional and supervised rather than as a triumphant homecoming; on the chastened character of the return as structurally load-bearing for the episode's resolution alongside the celestial humiliation itself.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The post-Kirimu narrative continuation in the wider epic presents the chastened hero as at last in a position to assume the chiefly office for which the early-and-middle epic's father-son conflict has been preparing him — the chiefly succession being narratively conditional on the hero's having undergone the celestial humiliation and on his having accepted the cosmographic limits the celestial powers have imposed — and the moral-developmental shape of the epic's overall arc is therefore the *maturation-through-humiliation* pattern that Biebuyck and Mateene 1969 identify as the narrative's didactic kernel and that Belcher 1999 reads as the load-bearing feature distinguishing the Mwindo material from the Eurasian combat-myth corpus.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the post-Kirimu narrative continuation, on the chiefly succession's narrative framing as conditional on the celestial humiliation, and on the maturation-through-humiliation pattern as the narrative's didactic kernel; Biebuyck & Mateene 1969 and Belcher 1999, ch. on Mwindo, on the maturation-through-humiliation reading as the load-bearing feature distinguishing the Mwindo material from the Eurasian combat-myth corpus.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Mwindo

The maturation-through-hubris framing has, like the covenant-broken framing of §I, no need of the chaoskampf category to make sense of itself: the hero's biographical arc, his accumulation of power, the foreshadowing of correction, the killing as transgressive over-reach, the celestial humiliation, the chastened return, and the chiefly succession are all internal to the Nyanga tradition's own moral-didactic and reciprocal-balance registers, and a hearer who reads the recorded performance within those registers does not require any concept of "primordial chaos" or "cosmos-establishing combat" to follow what happens or to take the moral point — the narrative is *complete* within its own categories, and the chaoskampf reading of the Kirimu episode is an *additional* analytic move performed from outside the Nyanga tradition by a reader whose reading apparatus has been formed elsewhere.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the completeness of the maturation-through-hubris framing within the Nyanga tradition's own categories and on the chaoskampf reading as an additional analytic move performed from outside the tradition; Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, translators' introduction and Kirimu episode passages on the self-sufficiency of the Nyanga tradition's framing for the recorded performance; Ballentine 2015, on the methodological discipline of separating area-specialist framings from comparative-typology overlays.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ballentine-2015 ; loc=on the methodological discipline of separating area-specialist framings from comparative typology

The Mwindo / Kirimu episode is therefore not an *inversion* of the chaoskampf typology in the sense of Bida (where the *outcome* is inverted: slaying ends rather than establishes prosperity), but an *inversion of moral valence*: the killing is presented as hubris rather than as order-making, the dragon's death is registered as a transgression that demands correction rather than as the cosmogonic or sovereignty-establishing act, and the moral evaluation is the precise contrary of the chaoskampf typology's standard valuation of the hero's slaying — and this moral inversion is more pointed than Bida's outcome inversion because it leaves no narrative-structural space for reading the killing as a chaoskampf event whose moral framing has been negotiated downward; the killing is the episode at which a *categorically different* reading of the dragon-killing motif is in play.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the moral-valence inversion of the Mwindo / Kirimu episode relative to the chaoskampf typology — the killing as hubris rather than as order-making, the death as transgression rather than as cosmos-establishment, and the moral evaluation as the precise contrary of the chaoskampf valuation; on the moral inversion as more pointed than Bida's outcome inversion in leaving no narrative-structural space for negotiated chaoskampf reading; per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml comparative_argument on the Mwindo material as the chapter's case of full refusal of the chaos-order narrative.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The chapter's reading of the Mwindo material as a *refusal* of the chaoskampf typology's moral-cosmographic apparatus, rather than as a member of an "inverse chaoskampf" subtype within that apparatus, follows the area-specialist tradition that Biebuyck and Mateene 1969 anchor and that Belcher 1999 develops within the comparative African-oral-epic apparatus: the Nyanga tradition's framing — maturation-through-hubris, reciprocal balance between human and spirit worlds, the sky-god's authority over the hero — is the tradition's own categorical apparatus, and reading the Kirimu episode as the inverse of a Eurasian combat-myth pattern would be to subordinate the Nyanga categorical apparatus to a comparative typology developed for materials whose categorical apparatus is structurally different.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the area-specialist reading of the Mwindo material as refusing the chaoskampf typology's moral-cosmographic apparatus rather than fitting within it as an "inverse chaoskampf" instance; Biebuyck & Mateene 1969 and Belcher 1999 on the Nyanga tradition's categorical apparatus (maturation-through-hubris, reciprocal balance, sky-god authority); per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" on the methodological status of comparativist-typological readings as additional moves rather than as primary readings of non-Eurasian materials.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=belcher-epic ; loc=Epic Traditions of Africa, ch. on Mwindo

[APPROPRIATION-FLAG: the area-specialist objection registered here is that the Mwindo / Kirimu episode's *own* framing within the Nyanga oral tradition is maturation-through-hubris — a moral-didactic and reciprocal-balance framing in which the hero's killing of the dragon is registered as an unsanctioned act of human power against the spirit world that the sky-god Nkuba punishes by lightning-seizing and celestial humiliation, and in which the killing's narrative function is to set up the hero's chastening and his eventual chastened assumption of the chiefly office — and not "inversion of cosmogony", a frame imported from the comparative typology of Eurasian combat-myth scholarship and applied to the Nyanga material from outside; the objection is more pointed than the Bida case (which is an inversion of *outcome* within a frame the Soninke tradition does not employ) because the Nyanga case is an inversion of *moral valence* on top of a categorical apparatus that the Nyanga tradition does not employ either, leaving the chaoskampf reading no negotiable space within the Nyanga tradition's own framing. | named_specialists: Daniel Biebuyck and Kahombo C. Mateene (*The Mwindo Epic from the Banyanga (Zaire)*, 1969, Kirimu episode and following celestial-journey passages; in sources.yaml as biebuyck-mateene-mwindo) are the principal area-specialist anchors for the maturation-through-hubris reading; Stephen Belcher (*Epic Traditions of Africa*, 1999, ch. on Mwindo; in sources.yaml as belcher-epic) is the principal comparative African-oral-epic synthesis anchor; the wider Nyanga-area area-specialist tradition associated with Biebuyck's later *Hero and Chief: Epic Literature from the Banyanga* (1978; named in body prose at Ch29 per the named-but-flagged secondary-anchor pattern) supplies the wider area-specialist scholarly tradition within which the Mwindo material is read. | tier_implication: scope.md classifies the Mwindo material as CONTRASTIVE-tier (full) precisely because the area-specialist framing refuses the chaoskampf typology's moral-cosmographic apparatus rather than accommodating it as an inverse-chaoskampf instance, and the contrastive-tier classification is itself a recognition of the area-specialists' priority and of the "resistance is the data" discipline that the Mwindo material exemplifies. | forwards_to: this APPROPRIATION-FLAG is engaged at length in the present comparative chapter (Ch40, the inversion problem) as the most pointed of the chapter's cases, and again in Ch41 (the refusal-and-appropriation problem), where the meta-question of what comparativism owes the area-specialists when the typology's own categories are refused is the chapter's organising question; the Mwindo case is the strongest case in the present book for the appropriation-protocol's substantive bite.]

The Mwindo episode's recording-history situation is itself worth registering at this point because it bears on the methodological discipline §II applies: the recorded performance is a single 1956 field encounter at Bese with the named narrator Shé-Kárisi Candi Rureke, made within the late-Belgian-colonial-period ethnographic context of the eastern Belgian Congo, with no parallel deep ethnographic-recording corpus across decades to triangulate against and with the colonial-ethnographic compositional-context filtering that the situation introduces — and current Mwindo-studies scholarship including Biebuyck's own subsequent reflective treatments and Belcher 1999 reads the 1956 Rureke text critically as a starting point rather than as a transparently-faithful record of pre-colonial Nyanga performance, a methodological caution that is itself part of the area-specialist citation policy this chapter applies.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the 1956 Bese recording's textual situation — single field encounter, late-Belgian-colonial-period context, no parallel deep ethnographic-recording corpus, colonial-ethnographic compositional-context filtering — and on the critical-reading discipline applied by current Mwindo-studies scholarship; Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, translators' introduction on the recording-history situation; Belcher 1999, ch. on Mwindo, on the critical-reading discipline.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=biebuyck-mateene-mwindo ; loc=The Mwindo Epic, episode of Kirimu

The methodological pay-off of presenting the maturation-through-hubris framing first, before invoking the chaoskampf-and-its-inversion overlay, is — as in §I — that the comparison the chapter performs is *more* precise rather than less: the chaoskampf typology is not asked to do work the Nyanga tradition refuses, the area-specialists' framing is not subordinated to a comparative apparatus the recorded performance does not employ, and the comparison between the Mwindo material and the Eurasian combat-myth corpus can proceed at the level of *categorical difference* (the Nyanga moral-cosmographic apparatus is structurally different from the chaoskampf moral-cosmographic apparatus) without forcing the comparison through a common categorical apparatus that would flatten the Nyanga material's distinctive contribution.footnote:[Per chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the methodological pay-off of presenting the maturation-through-hubris framing first; Ballentine 2015, on the methodological discipline of separating area-specialist framings from comparative-typology overlays as the route to more precise comparison; per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml special_instructions on the chapter's theoretical contribution being that allowing the area-specialists to speak first does not destroy the comparison but makes it more precise.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ballentine-2015 ; loc=on the methodological discipline of separating area-specialist framings from comparative typology

The contrast between Bida (§I) and Mwindo (§II) is itself instructive for the chapter's typological work: where Bida's case is the inversion of the *outcome* of the chaoskampf typology (slaying ends rather than establishes prosperity) within a covenant-broken framing that does not employ chaoskampf categories, Mwindo's case is the inversion of the *moral valence* within a maturation-through-hubris framing that not only does not employ chaoskampf categories but explicitly refuses the moral evaluation that the typology assigns to the hero's slaying — and the two cases together demonstrate that the typology's "inversion" subtype is not a single phenomenon but a cluster of structurally-different area-specialist framings that the comparativist's "inverse chaoskampf" label flattens together at the cost of the area-specialists' precision.footnote:[Per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml comparative_argument on the contrast between Bida's outcome-inversion and Mwindo's moral-valence-inversion as itself instructive for the chapter's typological work, and on the demonstration that the typology's "inversion" subtype is a cluster of structurally-different area-specialist framings rather than a single phenomenon; per chapters/28-bida-wagadu.claims.approved.adoc and chapters/29-mwindo-kirimu.claims.approved.adoc on the structurally-different area-specialist framings of the two cases.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=ballentine-2015 ; loc=on the typological discipline of separating area-specialist framings from comparative typology

// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C1. Combat as cosmogony (Ch34) — the order-establishing-by-combat structure of Marduk's combat with Tiamat, taken in Ch34 as the chaoskampf typology's strong-cosmogonic anchor, is the structural-comparative foil against which the Mwindo / Kirimu episode is read in §II above as a moral-valence-inverted case in which the killing is hubris rather than order-establishment; the Mwindo case is the chapter's most pointed refusal of the C1 subtype's moral-cosmographic apparatus.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C5. The IE HERO SLAY SERPENT formula (Ch38) — the IE-formula reading of dragon-slaying as a heroic deed-template that establishes the hero's standing, taken in Ch38 as the IE-comparative reading of Sigurd-Fáfnir, is contrasted in §II above with the Mwindo case in which the dragon-slaying establishes nothing (the hero is punished, not vindicated, by the slaying); the contrast prepares the §III treatment of Sigurd as the partial case at which the IE-formula reading and the long-arc tragedy of the cycle pull in different directions.
// COMPARATIVE-HOOK: C8. Refusal and appropriation (Ch41) — the Mwindo case is the present chapter's strongest case for the appropriation-protocol's substantive bite, and the C8 chapter develops the meta-question of what comparativism owes the area-specialists when the typology's own moral-cosmographic apparatus is refused; the Mwindo case is the inversion problem's pointed-end exemplar and the appropriation problem's anchor case.

=== III. The partial case — Sigurd, the IE formula, and the curse on the gold

Sigurd is the chapter's partial case, sitting on the edge of the IE-comparative tradition where Watkins's philological reading of the killing-stroke and the long-arc tragedy of the Sigurd-cycle pull the typology in different directions at different analytic levels.

The Old Norse Eddic Sigurd-cycle, preserved principally in the *Fáfnismál* of the Codex Regius (GKS 2365 4to, c. 1270 CE) — the second of the three closely-grouped Sigurd-cycle poems in the Codex (with *Reginsmál* preceding and *Sigrdrífumál* following) — and cited in the present chapter through Carolyne Larrington's revised Oxford World's Classics translation of *The Poetic Edda* (Oxford University Press, 2014), presents a partial case for the inversion problem: Watkins 1995 reads the *Fáfnismál*'s diction as the strongest philological reflex of the reconstructed Indo-European poetic formula HERO SLAY SERPENT in the entire Indo-European corpus, but the long-arc tragedy of the Sigurd-cycle complicates the order-establishing-by-combat reading that the formula's surface structure would suggest.footnote:[Per chapters/19-ie-reflexes.claims.approved.adoc on the *Fáfnismál* (Codex Regius GKS 2365 4to, c. 1270 CE) as the principal primary text for the Sigurd-Fáfnir narrative and on Larrington 2014 (rev. Oxford World's Classics) as the chapter's standard English-language translation; Watkins 1995, *How to Kill a Dragon: Aspects of Indo-European Poetics*, ch. 42 on the *Fáfnismál* diction as the strongest philological reflex of the reconstructed IE poetic formula HERO SLAY SERPENT in the entire IE corpus; per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml structure on Sigurd as the chapter's partial case at which the IE-formula reading and the long-arc tragedy of the cycle pull in different directions.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 42
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál

The narrative core of the *Fáfnismál* is the killing of the dragon Fáfnir by the young hero Sigurd, son of Sigmund of the Völsung lineage and foster-son of the smith Regin (Fáfnir's brother): Sigurd ambushes the dragon by digging a pit on the dragon's path to the water, lying in wait below, and thrusting upward into Fáfnir's underbelly with the sword Gram (forged by Regin from the fragments of Sigmund's broken sword) as the dragon passes over the pit; the killing-stroke is delivered as the central act around which the poem's dialogue is organised, with the bulk of the *Fáfnismál* given over to the post-killing wisdom-dialogue between the dying dragon and the hero rather than to extended combat-narrative.footnote:[Per chapters/19-ie-reflexes.claims.approved.adoc on the *Fáfnismál*'s narrative core — Sigurd's lineage and Regin's foster-role, the pit-and-ambush mechanism, the upward thrust with Gram into Fáfnir's underbelly, and the central placement of the killing-stroke around which the poem's dialogue is organised; Larrington 2014, *Fáfnismál* opening and middle stanzas, on the killing-stroke and on the post-killing wisdom-dialogue's bulk.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál opening

Watkins's IE-formula reading reads the *Fáfnismál*'s diction as a direct philological reflex of the reconstructed Indo-European poetic formula HERO SLAY SERPENT — the formula reconstructed across Vedic Indra-versus-Vṛtra material (where Watkins 1995 establishes the formula's reconstructed shape from the ​​Ṛgvedic diction), Greek and Iranian comparanda, and the Old Norse Eddic *Fáfnismál* itself — and the Eddic poet's choice of verb, object, and syntactic frame at the killing-stroke is the strongest philological evidence Watkins 1995 marshals for the formula's continued productivity at the diction-level in early-medieval Old Norse poetry; the Sigurd-Fáfnir killing is therefore an IE-poetic-formula reflex at the level of *language*, not just at the level of narrative motif.footnote:[Per chapters/19-ie-reflexes.claims.approved.adoc on Watkins's IE-formula reading of the *Fáfnismál* diction as a direct philological reflex of the reconstructed IE formula HERO SLAY SERPENT; Watkins 1995, ch. 42, on the Eddic poet's verb, object, and syntactic frame at the killing-stroke as the strongest philological evidence for the formula's productivity at the diction-level in early-medieval Old Norse poetry; on the Sigurd-Fáfnir killing as an IE-poetic-formula reflex at the level of language rather than just at the level of narrative motif.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 42

The IE-formula reading, taken on its own, reads the killing as a heroic deed-template event in which the hero's IE-typological standing is established by the slaying — and the surface narrative supports the reading: the *Fáfnismál* indicates that Sigurd, immediately after the killing, gains the speech of birds by tasting the dragon's heart's blood, kills the treacherous Regin in turn (warned by the birds of Regin's intent), takes Fáfnir's hoard onto his horse Grani, and proceeds to the next stage of his career (the encounter with the valkyrie Sigrdrífa / Brynhild in the *Sigrdrífumál*); the surface trajectory looks like vindication-of-the-hero by the slaying.footnote:[Per chapters/19-ie-reflexes.claims.approved.adoc on the post-killing surface trajectory — heart-roasting, blood-tasting, finger-burn, bird-speech, the nuthatches' warning, Sigurd's killing of Regin, the loading of the hoard onto Grani, and the bridge to the Sigrdrífa / Brynhild encounter; Larrington 2014, *Fáfnismál* (heart-roasting and bird-speech) and concluding stanzas, on the surface vindication-of-the-hero trajectory.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál (heart-roasting and bird-speech)

But the long-arc tragedy of the Sigurd-cycle complicates the surface reading: the gold of Andvari that Loki extorts to pay Hreidmar's wergild for the killing of Otr (Hreidmar's son and Fáfnir's and Regin's brother) is *cursed* by Andvari at the moment of the extortion, and the curse drives the entire downstream cycle — the death of Hreidmar at Fáfnir's hands, Fáfnir's transformation into a dragon by his obsessive guardianship of the cursed gold, Regin's treachery toward Sigurd, Sigurd's eventual death at the hands of Gunnar's brother Gutthorm in the Burgundian episode, Brynhild's death, and the wider Burgundian disasters — and the killing of the dragon, far from establishing Sigurd's prosperity, is the *acquisition of the curse* that destroys him and the people around him.footnote:[Per chapters/19-ie-reflexes.claims.approved.adoc on the long-arc tragedy of the Sigurd-cycle from *Reginsmál* through *Fáfnismál*, *Sigrdrífumál*, and the Burgundian poems to Sigurd's death — the curse on Andvari's gold as the load-bearing motif driving the cycle from the killing of the dragon to Sigurd's own death and the Burgundian disasters; Larrington 2014, *Reginsmál* and *Fáfnismál* on the curse's establishment; on the killing as the acquisition of the curse rather than the establishment of prosperity.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Reginsmál; Fáfnismál

The dying Fáfnir's own warning to Sigurd within the *Fáfnismál*'s post-killing dialogue is itself part of the *Fáfnismál*'s framing: Fáfnir, mortally wounded, asks Sigurd's name, warns him of the curse on the gold, and supplies cosmological-and-genealogical lore in the wisdom-dialogue register that the *Vafþrúðnismál* and *Grímnismál* establish elsewhere in the Eddic corpus, and the warning-of-the-curse is built into the killing-poem itself — the narrative does not require external commentary to register the killing as ambiguous; the dragon's own speech registers the act's tragic shape in the moment of its completion.footnote:[Per chapters/19-ie-reflexes.claims.approved.adoc on the post-killing wisdom-dialogue between Sigurd and the dying Fáfnir — Fáfnir's asking of Sigurd's name, the warning of the curse, the cosmological-and-genealogical lore in the wisdom-dialogue register — and on the warning-of-the-curse as built into the killing-poem itself; Larrington 2014, *Fáfnismál* stanzas (post-killing dialogue), on the wisdom-dialogue register paralleled in *Vafþrúðnismál* and *Grímnismál*.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Fáfnismál stanzas (post-killing dialogue)

The IE-formula reading and the curse-on-the-gold reading are not in straightforward conflict: the IE-formula reading is a *philological* claim about the diction of the killing-stroke, and the curse-on-the-gold reading is a *narrative* claim about the long-arc trajectory of the cycle, and the two readings are at different analytic levels; but the readings *are* in tension at the typological level, where the IE-formula's standard interpretation as a heroic-deed-template-establishing-the-hero's-standing reading runs against the cycle's narrative trajectory in which the killing acquires the curse that destroys the hero.footnote:[Per chapters/19-ie-reflexes.claims.approved.adoc on the analytic-level distinction between Watkins's philological IE-formula reading and the narrative-level long-arc tragedy reading, and on the typological-level tension between the heroic-deed-template-establishing-the-hero's-standing reading and the curse-acquiring-and-hero-destroying reading; Watkins 1995, ch. 42, on the IE-formula reading; Larrington 2014, on the long-arc tragedy.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 42
// EVIDENCE: source_id=larrington-poetic-edda ; loc=Sigurd-cycle long arc

The Sigurd case is therefore a *partial* inversion case: the killing is at one analytic level a paradigm IE dragon-slaying (Watkins's strongest philological reflex; the heroic-deed-template at the diction-level), and at another analytic level the acquisition of the curse that destroys the hero (the cycle's long-arc tragedy; the killing as the trigger of the cycle's catastrophic outcome) — and the case sits on the edge between the chaoskampf typology's order-establishing reading and the inversion-problem cases of §§I–II in a way that neither vindicates the typology's standard reading nor fully refuses it.footnote:[Per chapters/19-ie-reflexes.claims.approved.adoc on the Sigurd case as a partial inversion case sitting on the edge between the chaoskampf typology's order-establishing reading and the inversion-problem cases of §§I–II of the present chapter; per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml structure on Sigurd as the partial case at which the IE-formula reading and the long-arc tragedy of the cycle pull in different directions.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=ch. 42

The Sigurd case is also methodologically distinctive within the chapter: where Bida (§I), Mwindo (§II), Pariacaca (§IV), and Susanoo (§V) are non-Indo-European cases at which the area-specialist citation policy of scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" is in force, the Sigurd case is internal to the IE-comparative tradition that Watkins 1995 anchors and that Ch19 of the present book develops as the IE-reflexes chapter — and the appropriation-protocol's discipline of presenting the area-specialist framing first does not apply in the same way, because the Eddic philological tradition and the IE-comparative tradition are part of the *same* scholarly apparatus rather than being foreign to it; the Sigurd case is therefore *not* a case where comparativism imposes its categories on an outside tradition, and the inversion-problem reading at §III is correspondingly different in its methodological warrant from the readings at §§I–II and §§IV–V.footnote:[Per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" on the area-specialist citation requirement applying to non-Eurasian materials and to traditions outside the IE-comparative apparatus; per chapters/19-ie-reflexes.claims.approved.adoc on the Sigurd-cycle's place internal to the IE-comparative tradition that Watkins 1995 anchors; per brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml structure on Sigurd as a case methodologically distinct from §§I–II and §§IV–V because the Eddic philological tradition is internal to the IE-comparative apparatus rather than foreign to it.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=watkins-1995 ; loc=Part V

[INFERENCE: the Sigurd case's appropriate placement in the inversion-problem chapter is as a *partial* case rather than as either a vindication of the chaoskampf-typology reading (in which the killing establishes the hero's standing) or a full inversion case (in which the killing has the opposite valence): the philological IE-formula reading is robust and is the strongest evidence for the formula's productivity in early-medieval Old Norse poetry, but the long-arc tragedy of the cycle complicates the reading at the narrative level, and the appropriate registration of the case is as evidence that the chaoskampf typology and its inversion are themselves graded categories with edges rather than discrete bins. | basis: Watkins 1995 ch. 42 supplies the IE-formula reading at the philological level; Larrington 2014's *Fáfnismál* and the surrounding Codex Regius poems supply the long-arc tragedy at the narrative level; the brief 40-C7-inversion-problem.yaml structure explicitly characterises Sigurd as the chapter's partial case. | risk: the partial-case classification depends on accepting both the philological IE-formula reading and the long-arc tragedy reading at the analytic levels at which Watkins and Larrington respectively present them; alternative readings have been advanced (some Eddic scholars read the long-arc tragedy as compositional layering rather than as a unified cycle, which would weaken the curse-acquisition reading), and the partial-case classification is the chapter's reading rather than the only available scholarly position.]
